Conditional Wage in Animal Hire: The Hanafi Validation
Extension of same principle to animal hire with destination/load conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on conditional wage in animal hire and explain why Abu Hanifa validates the contract despite the ambiguity.

Core Idea
In hiring an animal, a conditional wage based on distance or load is valid: the lesser wage is due immediately.

This passage extends the earlier ruling on conditional wages from hiring a shop or tailor to hiring an animal (beast of burden). The contract specifies different wages for different destinations or loads. Abu Hanifa again validates the contract, while the Sahibayn, Zufar, and others reject it due to perceived ambiguity. Understanding this helps grasp the Hanafi approach to managing uncertainty in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual scenarios
Note the two variations: wage differs by destination (Baghdad vs. Kufa) and by load (oil vs. iron). The common feature is that the wage is unknown at inception but dependent on future performance.
These are not separate contracts but a single contract with conditional pricing.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds the contract valid (sahih). The lesser of the two wages is immediately due upon delivery; the additional amount is suspended and becomes due only if the more expensive option is actually performed.
This avoids gharar because the base wage is known.
3
Identify the opposing view and its basis
The Sahibayn, Zufar, and three others reject validity. They argue the final wage remains uncertain at contract formation, rendering the contract fasid due to gharar fahish.
Gharar here is excessive uncertainty about the consideration.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the minimum known wage and the contingent increment. The minimum is fixed and due; the increment is conditional on actual use and thus not part of the base exchange, so no gharar infects the core contract.
This reasoning relies on the principle that uncertainty in a subordinate element does not void the primary obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If Abu Hanifa's view is adopted: contract valid, lesser wage paid for actual destination/load, extra suspended. If opponents' view: contract fasid, the animal hire is void, and the hirer must pay the fair wage (ujr al-mithl) for the service rendered.
The practical outcome affects both parties' obligations.

Source Text

The text explicitly states the reasoning is what has preceded (al-wajh ma marr), referring back to the earlier argument about the lesser wage being immediately due and increase suspended.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa validates conditional wage contracts in animal hire by deeming the lesser wage due and the extra contingent, avoiding gharar.

Hirer Cannot Travel with Hired Slave Without Stipulation
Ruling on travel with a hired slave, with rational proof and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on traveling with a hired slave, explain the rationale based on custom ('urf) and harm, and identify the exceptions that permit travel.

Core Idea
The default hiring contract covers town service; travel requires stipulation or custom, otherwise it is prohibited.

In this lesson, we examine a key point in Hanafi hire law (ijarah): when can a hirer take the hired slave on a journey? The Kanz al-Daqaiq provides a clear rule with its rational proof. We will unpack the argument from custom, the principle of harm, and the exceptions that allow travel.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify that the hirer may not travel with the slave without a condition. The default service is considered to be in town (hadar).
2
Understand the rationale
Why is travel excluded? Two reasons: (a) custom: people commonly hire for town service, so the contract is understood accordingly; (b) hardship and expense: travel is harder and the return cost harms the owner.
The principle 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative) is at work.
3
Identify the exceptions
Travel is allowed if (a) the contract stipulates travel, or (b) at the time of hiring the slave was known to be prepared for travel (custom as implied condition).
The rule 'al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut' applies.
4
Know the consequence of violation
If the hirer travels without justification, he becomes a usurper (ghasib) and must compensate; he loses the right to wages even if the slave returns safely.
The principle 'al-daman wa al-ujrah la yajtami'an' (liability and wage cannot combine).
5
Apply the boundary
The hirer may still use the slave within the city and its outskirts (suburbs) and until late evening, as long as it does not constitute travel.
This is from the absolute nature of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب إجارة العبد
ولا يسافر بعبد استأجره للخدمة بلا شرط، ولا يأخذ المستأجر من عبد محجور المستأجر المسافرة أجرا دفعه لعمله، ولا يضمن غاصب العبد ما أكل . المستأجر أي لأجل عمله مفعول لا يضمن أي لا يسترد عليه آخر : نفسه
ولا يسافر بعبد إلخ أي إذا استأجر رجل عبدا للخدمة، ولم يشترط أنه يسافر به ولا كان متهيئا للسفر، فليس له أن يسافر به؛ لأن مطلق العقد تناول الخدمة في الحضر، وهو الأعم الأغلب، وعليه عرف الناس، فانصرف إليه، فلا يكون له أن ينقله إلى خدمة السفر؛ لأنه أشق، ولأن مؤونة الرد على المولى، فيلحقه ضرر بذلك، فلا يملكه إلا بإذنه، بخلاف العبد الموصى بخدمته حيث لا يتقيد بالحضر؛ لأن مؤونة الرد عليه، ولم يوجد العرف في حقه، ولأن التفاوت بين الخدمتين ،ظاهر، فإذا تعينت الخدمة في الحضر لا يبقى غيره داخلا كما في الركوب. وأما إذا شرط المسافرة، أو كان وقت الإجارة متهيئا للسفر، وعرف به له أن يسافر به؛ لأن الشرط ملزم، والمعروف كالمشروط، ولو سافر به ضمن؛ لأنه صار غاصبا ولا أجر عليه وإن سلم؛ لأن الضمان والأجر لا يجتمعان، وقيد بقوله: ولا يسافر؛ ليفيد أن له أن يستعمل فيما دون السفر كما استأجر عبدا للخدمة ولم يبين مكان الخدمة له أن يستخدمه في المصر ويخرجه إلى القرى وأفنية المصر، ولا يسافر به وله أن يستخدمه إلى العشاء الأخيرة، ويكلفه أنواع الخدمة ويخدم ضيفانه وامرأته لإطلاقه الفتح والتكملة وغيرهما باختصار)
Chapter on Hiring of a Slave
And he should not travel with a slave whom he hired for service without a condition, and the hirer should not take from a restricted slave—the hirer, traveling—a wage paid for his work, and a usurper of a slave is not liable for what he ate. The hirer, i.e., for his work; object of 'he is not liable'; i.e., he does not recover. Another [reading]: himself.
'And he should not travel with a slave' etc., i.e., if a man hired a slave for service and did not stipulate that he will travel with him, nor was he (the slave) prepared for travel, then he may not travel with him; because the absolute contract covers service in the town, which is more common and prevalent, and upon it is the custom of people, so it is understood (to mean that). Thus he may not transfer him to service of travel because it is harder, and because the expense of return is upon the owner (master), so harm would befall him by that. So he does not own (the right) except with his permission. Contrary to a slave bequeathed for service where it is not restricted to the town, because the expense of return is upon (the legatee? the text may be unclear), and custom is not established in his favor, and because the difference between the two services is apparent. So when service in the town is determined, nothing else remains included, as in the case of riding. But if he stipulated travel, or at the time of hiring the slave was prepared for travel and known for it, he may travel with him; because the condition is binding, and what is well-known is like what is stipulated. If he travels with him without such, he becomes liable (as a usurper) and no wage is due to him even if he returns, because liability and wage do not combine. He restricted by saying 'he should not travel' to indicate that he may employ him in what is less than travel, just as if he hired a slave for service and did not specify the place of service, he may use him in the city and take him out to villages and suburbs of the city, but not travel with him. And he may use him until the late evening prayer, and charge him with various types of service, and he (the slave) should serve his guests and wife, due to the absolute nature (of the contract). (Taken from Fath, Takmilah, and others, abridged).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on hiring slaves. It presents three rulings; the first concerns travel. 

Ruling: When a person hires a slave for general service without stipulating travel, he cannot take the slave on a journey out of town. The reason: The default understanding ('urf) of an absolute hire contract is service within the town (hadar). Travel is harder and imposes additional expenses (return cost) on the slave's owner, causing harm. The hirer cannot unilaterally increase the burden.

Exception: If travel was explicitly stipulated, or if at the time of contract the slave was known to be prepared for travel (e.g., a slave who regularly traveled with previous hirers), then travel is allowed because the custom serves as an implicit condition.

Consequence: If the hirer travels without such justification, he becomes a usurper (ghasib) of the slave. He must pay compensation for any harm, and he loses the right to any wage (ujrah) even if the slave returns safely, because liability (damān) and wage cannot combine.

Boundary: The hirer may still use the slave within the town and its outskirts (villages, suburbs) and even require service until late evening, as long as it does not constitute travel (i.e., overnight journey). This is from the absolute nature of the contract.

Key Terms
الخدمة (al-khidmah): General personal service; the default benefit in a slave-hiring contract.
السفر (al-safar): Travel; a more burdensome service that is not included without stipulation or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When may the hirer travel with a hired slave?
A) Whenever he wishes, as long as the slave is returned
B) Only if stipulated in the contract or the slave is known for travel
C) If he pays extra wage
D) Never, because slaves cannot be taken out of town

Key Takeaway
Travel with a hired slave is prohibited without explicit stipulation or established custom, reflecting the importance of 'urf and the principle of avoiding harm.

Hirer Cannot Recover Wage from a Restricted Slave
Ruling with proof using istihsan and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recovering a wage paid to a restricted slave and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides the apparent qiyas.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits the wage to remain with the slave if work is completed safely, overriding the strict qiyas of invalid contract.

This lesson examines a key application of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh: the case of a hirer who pays a wage to a restricted slave (mahjur). Although the basic analogy (qiyas) suggests the contract is void and the hirer can recover his money, istihsan considers the beneficial outcome and prevents recovery. This ruling reflects the school's concern for preventing hardship and upholding transactions that yield benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act in question is the hirer's payment of a wage to a mahjur slave. The ruling is that the hirer cannot recover the wage (istihsan) but could under qiyas.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Follow the qiyas reasoning: since the mahjur's contract is invalid, the wage remains the hirer's property; the hirer is a usurper of the slave's services and must pay compensation if the slave dies, but benefits of a usurped item are not compensable, so the wage stays with the hirer.
Qiyas stems from the principle that an invalid contract does not transfer ownership.
3
Grasp the istihsan justification
Istihsan looks at the actual outcome: if the slave works safely, the action is beneficial and effectively permitted, so the wage leaves the hirer's ownership. Thus recovery is blocked.
The basis is that the law permits beneficial acts even from those under restriction.
4
Apply the parallel case of a restricted minor
Note that the same ruling applies to a restricted minor who hires himself out; if he completes work safely, he keeps the wage. This reinforces the istihsan principle.
5
Consider the exception: slave perishes
If the slave dies during use, the hirer must pay the slave's value and no wage is due. This shows the balance between daman (liability) and ujrah (wage).
The two cannot combine: either wage is kept (if safe) or value is paid (if perished).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يأخذ إلخ: يعني لو استأجر رجل عبدا محجورا عليه من نفسه، فعمل، وأعطاه الأجر، ليس للمستأجر أن يسترد ما أعطاه استحسانا والقياس له أن يأخذه منه؛ لأن عقد المحجور عليه لا يجوز، فيبقى على ملك المستأجر؛ لأنه بالاستعمال صار غاصبا له ولهذا يجب عليه ضمان قيمته إذا هلك، ومنافع المغصوب لا تضمن عندنا، فيبقى المدفوع على ملكه، فله أن يسترده.
وجه الاستحسان: أن التصرف نافع على اعتبار الفراغ من العمل سالما ضارا على اعتبار هلاك العبد، والنافع مأذون فيه، فيجوز، فتخرج الأجرة عن ملكه، فليس له أن يسترده، وكذا الصبي المحجور عليه إذا آجر نفسه، وسلم من العمل كان الأجر له؛ لأنه غير ممنوع عما ينفعه من التصرفات وإذا هلك العبد في حالة الاستعمال يجب على المستأجر قيمته ولا يجب عليه الأجر. (فتح وتكملة)
And he should not take etc.: i.e., if a man hired a slave who is restricted (mahjur) over himself, and he worked, and he gave him the wage, the hirer cannot recover what he gave, by istihsan. By qiyas he could take it from him, because the contract of a restricted person is not valid, so it remains the property of the hirer, because by using him he became a usurper of him, and therefore he is liable for his value if he perishes. And the benefits of a usurped thing are not compensable according to us, so the paid amount remains his ownership, so he may recover it.
The basis of istihsan: The action (the working) is beneficial considering the completion of the work safely, and harmful considering the perishing of the slave. And the beneficial (action) is permitted, so it is valid. Thus the wage leaves his ownership, so he cannot recover it. Similarly, a restricted minor if he hires himself out and completes the work safely, the wage is his; because he is not prevented from transactions that benefit him. And if the slave perishes during use, the hirer must pay his value and the wage is not due (on the hirer). (From Fath, Takmilah).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage gives the second ruling in the chapter: a hirer who pays a wage to a restricted slave (mahjur) cannot recover that wage, by istihsan. 

The 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) would suggest that the contract of a mahjur is invalid, so the wage remains the property of the hirer; he is a usurper of the slave's services and must pay compensation for value if the slave dies, but since benefits of a usurped item are not guaranteed (madhhab), the paid wage remains with the hirer, so he can recover it.

However, istihsan (juristic preference) overrides this. The reasoning: The act of working is beneficial when the slave completes it safely. Such beneficial acts are effectively permitted (ma'dhun fih) by the law, even if the contract is technically invalid, because the slave is in a position to benefit himself. Thus the wage leaves the hirer's ownership, and he cannot recover it.

Comparison: The same applies to a restricted minor who hires himself out; if he completes work safely, he keeps the wage. But if the slave dies during use, the hirer pays his value and no wage is owed.

This shows Hanafi istihsan in action: overriding strict qiyas to prevent hardship and to uphold the benefit of the transaction when harm does not occur.

Key Terms
المحجور عليه (al-mahjur 'alayhi): A person under legal restriction (e.g., a slave or minor) who cannot conduct contracts independently.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for compensation; here, the hirer must pay value of slave if he dies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis (wajh) of istihsan that prevents the hirer from recovering the wage?
A) The slave's consent
B) The beneficial nature of the work if completed safely
C) The hirer's generosity
D) The slave's ownership of himself

Key Takeaway
Istihsan can override qiyas to give effect to a beneficial transaction, preventing recovery of a wage paid to a restricted person who completed work safely.

Usurper of a Slave is Not Liable for Wages Consumed
Ruling on usurper's liability for slave's earned wages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a usurper's liability for a slave's wages and explain the key concept of ihraz (protected possession) that underpins the ruling.

Core Idea
Liability for usurped property requires the property to be in the owner's protected possession; a slave's wage in the usurper's hand is not muhraz for the owner.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario within the chapter of usurpation (ghasb): when a usurper takes a slave, the slave earns wages through hiring himself out, and the usurper consumes those wages. Does the usurper owe compensation? The Hanafi masters dispute this. By understanding the concept of muhraz property, you will see how Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn derive different conclusions.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the dispute
Identify the factual situation: a usurper takes a slave, the slave hires himself out and earns a wage, the usurper takes and consumes that wage. The question is whether the usurper must pay compensation (daman) to the owner.
The dispute is between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
2
Identify the Sahibayn's argument
The Sahibayn hold the usurper liable because he destroyed someone else's property (the wage) without permission, and there is no justification (ta'wil) for his act. Their general principle is that destroying another's property triggers liability.
They see the wage as the property of the owner (since the slave's earnings belong to the owner).
3
Identify Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Abu Hanifa accepts the general principle but adds a condition: liability only arises if the destroyed property was 'muhraz' (protected, i.e., in the owner's secure possession or that of his deputy). He argues the wage here is not muhraz.
The condition of ihraz is a key element of Abu Hanifa's reasoning.
4
Analyze the definition of ihraz
Ihraz means the property is in the owner's hand or the hand of his deputy (na'ib). Apply to this case: the wage is in the hand of the slave, but the slave is not a deputy of the owner; rather, the slave is in the hand of the usurper. Thus, the wage is not in the owner's or his deputy's hand, so it is not muhraz.
The usurper's possession of the slave does not make the slave's earnings muhraz for the owner.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi position
According to the matn (and thus the relied-upon view), Abu Hanifa's position prevails: the usurper is not liable. The key is that the wage is not protected property for the owner.
This ruling illustrates the importance of ihraz in Hanafi tort law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن إلخ معناه : إذا غصب رجل عبدا، فآجر العبد نفسه فأخذ الغاصب من يد العبد الأجرة، فأكلها، فلا ضمان عليه عند الإمام وقالا عليه ضمانه؛ لأنه أتلف مال الغير بغير إذنه، ولا تأويل له، وللإمام أن الضمان إنما يجب بإتلاف مال محرز متقوم، وهذا ليس بمحرز؛ لأن الإحراز يكون بيده أو بيد ،نائبه، وهذا ليس في يده، ولا يد نائبه؛ لأن الغاصب ليس بنائب عنه ولا العبد، بل العبد وما في يده في يد الغاصب، فلم يكن محرزا، فلا ضمان، =
And he is not liable etc.: its meaning: if a man usurps a slave, then the slave hires himself out, and the usurper takes from the hand of the slave the wage and consumes it, there is no liability on him according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa). But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is liable for it, because he destroyed the property of another without permission and there is no interpretation (in his favor). For the Imam: Liability is incurred only by destroying property that is protected (muhraz) and of value. And this (wage) is not protected because protection (ihraz) is by being in his hand or in the hand of his deputy, and this is not in his hand nor in the hand of his deputy. Because the usurper is not his deputy, nor is the slave (his deputy). Rather, the slave and what is in his hand are in the hand of the usurper, so it was not protected, hence no liability.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the third ruling: A usurper (ghasib) who takes a slave and then takes the wage the slave earned and eats it is not liable for that wage according to Imam Abu Hanifa. 

Abu Hanifa's argument: Liability (daman) only applies to property that is 'muhraz' (protected, i.e., in the secure possession of its owner or his representative). Here, the wage is in the hand of the slave, but the slave is himself in the hand of the usurper. The slave is not a deputy (na'ib) of the owner, and the usurper is not a deputy. So the wage is not considered 'protected' property for the owner; it is in the hands of the wrongdoer (usurper) but that does not constitute ihraz for the owner. Thus, when the usurper consumes it, he is not liable to the owner.

The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: They hold the usurper liable because he destroyed someone else's property (the wage belonged to the slave? Actually belonged to the owner? The text says 'property of another' - but the wage after being paid to the slave belongs to the slave (see previous ruling). However, the slave is under guardianship, so the wage might still be considered as owned by the owner? The text is slightly ambiguous. The point is that the usurper has no right to take it.

The relied-upon position in the matn appears to be Abu Hanifa's, as the text introduces it with 'And he is not liable' and elaborates his reasoning.

Key Terms
المحرز (al-muhraz): Property that is protected by being in the secure possession of the owner or his agent.
الغاصب (al-ghasib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes possession of another's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the wages are not muhraz for the owner
C) Because the usurper becomes the de facto owner of the slave
D) Because the wage is less than ten dirhams

Key Takeaway
Liability for usurped property or its proceeds hinges on the property being in the owner's protected possession (muhraz); a slave's wage in the usurper's hand is not muhraz, so no compensation is due.

Traveling with a Hired Slave
Ruling with proof on traveling with a hired slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on traveling with a hired slave and explain the role of stipulation and custom (urf) in determining the scope of the contract.

Core Idea
A hired slave's service is presumed local; travel requires explicit stipulation or customary readiness.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling in ijarah: whether a hirer may take a hired slave on a journey. The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains that the default scope of a service contract is limited to settled areas, based on custom (urf) and the principle of avoiding harm. We will explore the conditions under which travel becomes permissible and the consequences of unauthorized travel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The act of traveling with a hired slave without stipulation is prohibited; the foundation is custom (urf) and harm (dharar) to the owner.
The ruling is haram according to the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the role of custom (urf)
Custom interprets 'service' as local service unless otherwise agreed; this restricts the contract to settled areas.
The text states: 'وعليه عرف الناس' (upon it is the custom of people).
3
Recognize exceptions
Travel becomes permissible if explicitly stipulated or if the slave was known for travel at the time of hiring. Also, a bequeathed slave is not restricted.
Exceptions are based on the binding nature of conditions and the well-known being like the stipulated.
4
Apply the consequence: ghasb
If travel occurs without right, the hirer becomes a usurper (ghasib) and is liable for the slave's value, but no wage is due even if the slave returns safely.
Liability (daman) and wage (ujrah) do not combine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسافر بعبد استأجره للخدمة بلا شرط، ولا يسافر بعبد إلخ أي إذا استأجر رجل عبدا للخدمة، ولم يشترط أنه يسافر به ولا كان متهيئا للسفر، فليس له أن يسافر به؛ لأن مطلق العقد تناول الخدمة في الحضر، وهو الأعم الأغلب، وعليه عرف الناس، فانصرف إليه، فلا يكون له أن ينقله إلى خدمة السفر؛ لأنه أشق، ولأن مؤونة الرد على المولى، فيلحقه ضرر بذلك، فلا يملكه إلا بإذنه، بخلاف العبد الموصى بخدمته حيث لا يتقيد بالحضر؛ لأن مؤونة الرد عليه، ولم يوجد العرف في حقه، ولأن التفاوت بين الخدمتين ظاهر، فإذا تعينت الخدمة في الحضر لا يبقى غيره داخلا كما في الركوب. وأما إذا شرط المسافرة، أو كان وقت الإجارة متهيئا للسفر، وعرف به له أن يسافر به؛ لأن الشرط ملزم، والمعروف كالمشروط، ولو سافر به ضمن؛ لأنه صار غاصبا ولا أجر عليه وإن سلم؛ لأن الضمان والأجر لا يجتمعان، وقيد بقوله: ولا يسافر؛ ليفيد أن له أن يستعمل فيما دون السفر كما استأجر عبدا للخدمة ولم يبين مكان الخدمة له أن يستخدمه في المصر ويخرجه إلى القرى وأفنية المصر، ولا يسافر به وله أن يستخدمه إلى العشاء الأخيرة، ويكلفه أنواع الخدمة ويخدم ضيفانه وامرأته لإطلاقه (الفتح والتكملة وغيرهما باختصار)
He may not travel with a slave he hired for service without a condition. Explanation: If a man hires a slave for service and did not stipulate that he may travel with him, nor was he ready for travel, then he may not travel with him because the absolute contract encompasses service in settled areas, which is the more common and predominant, and upon it is the custom of people, so it is restricted to that. Thus he may not transfer him to travel service because it is harder, and because the burden of return is on the owner, so harm befalls him by that, so he does not own it except with his permission. Unlike a slave bequeathed for service where it is not restricted to settled areas because the burden of return is on the heir, and custom does not apply in his case, and the difference between the two services is apparent. Once service in settled areas is specified, nothing else remains included, as in riding. And if he stipulates travel, or at the time of hiring he was ready for travel and known for it, he may travel with him because a condition is binding and the well-known is like the stipulated. And if he travels with him, he is liable because he becomes a usurper, and no wage is due even if he returns safely, because liability and wage do not combine. And the phrase 'he may not travel' implies that he may use him for less than travel, as when he hires a slave for service without specifying the place, he may use him in the city and take him to villages and outskirts, but not travel with him; and he may use him until the last evening prayer, and assign him various types of service, and serve his guests and wife, as stated in Fath and Takmila and others briefly.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding traveling with a hired slave. The author explains that when a slave is hired for service without any condition about travel, the contract is understood to cover only service in settled areas (hadar), because this is what the word 'service' ordinarily implies in custom (urf). The jurist argues that travel service is more burdensome and that the owner would suffer harm from the added expense of return, so the hirer cannot force it without the owner's permission. An exception is made for a slave whose service was bequeathed in a will, since the burden of return falls on the legatee and no custom restricts the service. If travel is explicitly stipulated, or if the slave was known at the time of hiring to be prepared for travel, then traveling is allowed because conditions are binding and custom has the force of a condition. If the hirer travels despite the prohibition, he becomes a usurper (ghasib) and must pay the slave's value if lost, but no wage is due even if the slave returns safely, because liability and wage cannot coexist. The explanation also notes that the prohibition on travel does not prevent the hirer from using the slave for lesser trips within the city or nearby villages, or for various household tasks, as detailed in Fath and Takmila.

Key Terms
الخدمة (al-khidmah): Service performed by a hired slave, understood in the contract as service in settled areas unless specified otherwise.
المسافرة (al-musāfarah): Traveling with the hired slave, which requires explicit stipulation or customary readiness.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation; taking the slave without right, triggering liability for the slave's value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a hirer travel with a hired slave without the owner's permission?
A) Always, because hiring includes all types of service.
B) Only if travel is explicitly stipulated or the slave was known for travel at hiring.
C) Only if the hirer pays extra wage.
D) Only if the owner is absent.

Key Takeaway
Travel with a hired slave is prohibited without explicit stipulation or customary readiness due to custom and harm principle.

Wages Paid to a Mahjur Slave: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling with proof on recovery of wages paid to a mahjur slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recovery of wages paid to a mahjur slave and explain how istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Istihsan validates a mahjur slave's beneficial transaction; the wage is his, not recoverable.

This lesson examines a ruling where a slave under interdiction (mahjur) is hired and completes work. By qiyas, the contract is void, but Hanafi jurists apply istihsan to uphold the wage payment. Understanding this distinction clarifies how beneficial acts are permitted for those under interdiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Consider the scenario: a man hires a mahjur slave, the slave works and receives a wage. The ruling by istihsan is that the hirer cannot recover the wage; it becomes the slave's property.
The default qiyas would rule the contract void, allowing recovery.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Qiyas holds the contract invalid because a mahjur cannot contract. Therefore, the wage remains the hirer's property, and he may recover it. The hirer is considered a usurper of the slave's services.
Usurpation would require payment of the slave's value if he dies.
3
Understand the istihsan argument
Istihsan considers that the transaction is beneficial if the work is completed safely. Since beneficial acts are permitted for a mahjur, the contract is valid, and the wage leaves the hirer's ownership.
The harm (death) is contingent and not realized.
4
Note the exception
If the slave dies during the work, the hirer must pay his value (due to usurpation) but no wage is owed. This shows the balanced consideration of benefit and harm.
5
Extend to analogous cases
The same ruling applies to a minor under interdiction who hires himself out and completes work safely.
Both are mahjur and benefit from istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يأخذ إلخ: يعني لو استأجر رجل عبدا محجورا عليه من نفسه، فعمل، وأعطاه الأجر، ليس للمستأجر أن يسترد ما أعطاه استحسانا والقياس له أن يأخذه منه؛ لأن عقد المحجور عليه لا يجوز، فيبقى على ملك المستأجر؛ لأنه بالاستعمال صار غاصبا له ولهذا يجب عليه ضمان قيمته إذا هلك، ومنافع المغصوب لا تضمن عندنا، فيبقى المدفوع على ملكه، فله أن يسترده. وجه الاستحسان: أن التصرف نافع على اعتبار الفراغ من العمل سالما ضارا على اعتبار هلاك العبد، والنافع مأذون فيه، فيجوز، فتخرج الأجرة عن ملكه، فليس له أن يسترده، وكذا الصبي المحجور عليه إذا آجر نفسه، وسلم من العمل كان الأجر له؛ لأنه غير ممنوع عما ينفعه من التصرفات وإذا هلك العبد في حالة الاستعمال يجب على المستأجر قيمته ولا يجب عليه الأجر. (فتح وتكملة)
And he does not take... meaning: If a man hires a slave who is under interdiction (mahjur) from himself, and the slave works and the hirer gives him the wage, the hirer cannot recover what he gave by istihsan, but by qiyas he may take it back because the contract of a mahjur is invalid, so the wage remains the property of the hirer; because by using the slave he becomes a usurper and thus must pay his value if he dies, and the benefits of a usurped item are not compensable in our school, so the paid amount remains his property, so he may recover it. The basis of istihsan: the transaction is beneficial considering the completion of work safely, and harmful considering the slave's death, and what is beneficial is permitted, so it is valid, thus the wage leaves his ownership, so he cannot recover it. Similarly, a child under interdiction if he hires himself out and completes the work safely, the wage is his because he is not prevented from beneficial transactions. And if the slave dies while being used, the hirer must pay his value but no wage is due.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof on the issue of wages paid to a slave who is under interdiction (mahjur). The legal problem is that a mahjur slave cannot validly contract, so qiyas (analogy) would hold the contract void and allow the hirer to recover the wage. However, the Hanafi position, based on istihsan (juristic preference), rules that the hirer cannot recover the wage if the work was completed safely. The reasoning is that the transaction is beneficial to the slave (he earns) and harmful only in case of death, and beneficial acts are permitted for a mahjur. The same applies to a minor under interdiction. If the slave dies during the work, the hirer must pay the slave's value (because of usurpation) but no wage is due. This istihsan overrides the general qiyas. The passage references Fath and Takmila as sources.

Key Terms
عبد محجور عليه (ʿabd maḥjūr ʿalayhi): A slave under interdiction (hajr), prevented from conducting transactions.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas based on benefit, custom, or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, by qiyas the contract is void.
B) No, by istihsan the wage is validly owned by the slave.
C) Only if the slave dies.
D) Only if the hirer proves fraud.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan allows beneficial transactions for a mahjur; thus, wages paid for completed work are not recoverable.

Usurper's Liability for Slave's Wages
Intra-Hanafi dispute on usurper's liability for consumed wages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a usurper's liability for consuming a slave's wages and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A usurper is not liable for consuming a slave's wages because the wage is not guarded (muhraz) property.

This passage examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if a usurper takes a slave, the slave hires himself out, and the usurper consumes the wages, is he liable? Imam Abu Hanifa says no, while the Sahibayn say yes. The disagreement hinges on whether the wage qualifies as 'guarded' (muhraz) property, a key condition for liability in usurpation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa: the usurper is not liable for the consumed wages.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the usurper is liable because he destroyed another's property without permission.
Their argument is based on the general principle of daman (liability) for destruction.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn's proof: the usurper consumed property that belongs to the owner; therefore, he must compensate.
This is a straightforward application of the rule 'al-ghurm bil-ghunm' but not used here.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Imam Abu Hanifa responds that liability only attaches to property that is guarded (muhraz). The wage was in the usurper's own hand via the slave, so it lacks ihraz.
He analogizes to stolen goods after amputation: the thief no longer possesses them in a guarded manner.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Imam Abu Hanifa, the usurper keeps the consumed wage; under the Sahibayn, he must pay its value.
This highlights the importance of the condition of ihraz in Hanafi law of usurpation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن إلخ معناه : إذا غصب رجل عبدا، فآجر العبد نفسه فأخذ الغاصب من يد العبد الأجرة، فأكلها، فلا ضمان عليه عند الإمام وقالا عليه ضمانه؛ لأنه أتلف مال الغير بغير إذنه، ولا تأويل له، وللإمام أن الضمان إنما يجب بإتلاف مال محرز متقوم، وهذا ليس بمحرز؛ لأن الإحراز يكون بيده أو بيد نائبه، وهذا ليس في يده، ولا يد نائبه؛ لأن الغاصب ليس بنائب عنه ولا العبد، بل العبد وما في يده في يد الغاصب، فلم يكن محرزا، فلا ضمان، فصار نظير المال المسروق في يد السارق بعد القطع، ولأن الأجرة بدل المنفعة، والبدل حكمه حكم المبدل، ولو أتلف الغاصب المنفعة لا يضمن فكذا بدلها وما تردد بين أصلين توفر حظهما، فرجحنا جانب المالك عند بقاء الأجر في يده، فقلنا: المالك أحق به، ورجحنا جانب الغاصب في حق الضمان وقلنا لا ضمان عليه إذا أكل الأجرة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٦/٨
And he does not guarantee... meaning: If a man usurps a slave, and the slave hires himself out, and the usurper takes the wage from the slave's hand and consumes it, then there is no liability upon him according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say there is liability because he destroyed the property of another without permission, and there is no justification. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, liability is only obligatory for destroying property that is guarded (muhraz) and valuable, and this (the wage) is not guarded because being guarded requires it to be in his hand or in the hand of his agent, and this is not in his hand nor in the hand of his agent; because the usurper is not an agent for him, nor is the slave, rather the slave and what is in his hand is in the hand of the usurper, so it was not guarded, so no liability. Thus it is analogous to stolen property in the hand of the thief after amputation. And because the wage is the substitute for the benefit, and the substitute has the same ruling as the original, and if the usurper destroys the benefit he is not liable, so likewise its substitute. And since it is contested between two principles, we give both their due: we favor the owner's side regarding the wage when it remains in his hand, so we say the owner has more right to it, and we favor the usurper's side regarding liability, so we say no liability when he consumes the wage.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a usurper (ghasib) who takes a slave's wages and consumes them. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the usurper is not liable for the consumed wage, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he is liable. The Imam's reasoning is that liability for destruction of property only applies if the property is 'muhraz' (guarded/in protected possession). Here, the wage in the slave's hand is not considered guarded because the slave and his hand are under the usurper's control, analogous to stolen goods after the hand is cut off (i.e., no longer in the thief's possession). Furthermore, the wage is a substitute for the benefit of the slave's work, and since the usurper is not liable for destroying the benefit itself (as benefits are not compensable in usurpation), the substitute similarly is not compensable. The text notes that the ruling balances between two principles: the owner's right to the wage if it remains, and the usurper's immunity from liability if he consumes it. This dispute is recorded in Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): A person who takes someone else's property without right, in this case a slave.
محرز (muḥraz): Property that is in protected possession, i.e., in the hand of the owner or his agent.
بدل المنفعة (badal al-manfaʿah): The substitute (wage) for the benefit (work) of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave is not his property.
B) Because the wage is not considered guarded (muhraz) property.
C) Because the wage is less than the slave's value.
D) Because the usurper did not intend to destroy.

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that a usurper is not liable for consumed wages is based on the condition that the wage was not in the owner's guarded possession, a principle that limits liability in usurpation.

Master Taking Wage from Slave's Hand and Validity of Slave's Acceptance
Rulings on master taking wage and slave's acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings: the master may take the wage from the slave's hand, and the slave's acceptance of the wage is valid, and explain why the slave's acceptance discharges the hirer.

Core Idea
Slave's wage belongs to master; but slave's own acceptance of wage is valid and frees the hirer.

This lesson covers two related rulings from the Hanafi school concerning the wages of a hired slave. First, if the master finds the wage in the slave's hand, he may take it because the slave's earnings are the master's property. Second, when the slave himself accepts the wage from the hirer, that acceptance is valid by consensus, and the hirer is discharged from liability. Understanding these rules clarifies the rights of the master and the capacity of the slave in financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling
The master finds the wage in the slave's hand. What is the ruling? The master takes it because the wage is his property.
The slave's earning capacity does not affect ownership.
2
Identify the second ruling
The slave accepts his own wage from the hirer. Is this valid? Yes, by consensus, because accepting a benefit is like accepting a gift.
The slave is the direct party to the hire contract.
3
Understand the effect of valid acceptance
When the slave validly accepts the wage, the hirer is discharged from the obligation to pay. This is unlike when the master hires out the slave, where payment must go to the master or his agent.
The benefit of validity is the hirer's discharge.
4
Compare with other scenarios
If the master hires out the slave, the slave cannot accept payment; only the master or his agent can. Here, the slave hires himself out, so he is the contracting party.
Contrast with the case where the master is the contracting party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أجره، ولو وجده ربه أخذه، وصح قبض العبد أجره، ولو آجر عبده هذين الشهرين شهرا بأربعة، وشهرا بخمسة صح، والأول بأربعة، ولو اختلفا في إباق العبد ومرضه حكم الحال،
For whoever hires him, and if his master finds (the wage), he takes it. And the slave's acceptance of his wage is valid. Explanation: He takes it, meaning what is in the slave's hand of the wage, because it is his property, and the invalidity of value does not necessitate invalidity of ownership. And the slave's acceptance of his wage is valid because it is purely beneficial and permitted, like accepting a gift. That is, when the slave hires himself out and the hirer gives him the wage, his acceptance is valid by consensus because he is the direct party to the contract, and the rights of the contract belong to him, so it is valid because he is permitted for beneficial transactions. The benefit of valid acceptance appears regarding the hirer's discharge from liability for the wage, as he is discharged by paying to the slave, unlike if the master hired him out, then payment is only valid with the master's agency because the master is the contracting party.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage consists of two related rulings. First, if a slave earns a wage, and his master finds that wage in the slave's hand, the master may take it because the slave's earnings are the master's property. The fact that the slave may be of no value (if he is a minor or interdicted) does not invalidate ownership. Second, the slave's own acceptance of the wage is valid by consensus, because accepting a beneficial payment is like accepting a gift. The practical effect is that the hirer is discharged from the obligation to pay when he pays the slave directly, unlike when the master hires out the slave, in which case payment must be made to the master or his agent. These rulings are from Fath and Takmila.

Key Terms
قبض العبد (qabḍ al-ʿabd): The slave taking possession of the wage; legally valid as beneficial act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because the hirer insists.

Key Takeaway
The master may take the slave's wage, and the slave's acceptance of his own wage is valid, discharging the hirer.

Hiring for Two Months at Different Rates
Ruling with proof on validity of hiring for two months with different rates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hiring for two months with different rates and explain the principle of taḥarrīyan li al-ṣiḥḥa used to validate the contract.

Core Idea
A hire contract with different rates for two months is valid; the first rate applies to the first month to avoid indefiniteness.

In hire contracts (ijara), specifying the period is essential. But what if the contract mentions two months with different rates without explicitly assigning rates to months? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq shows how Hanafi jurists use a powerful interpretive principle—taḥarrīyan li al-ṣiḥḥa (seeking validity)—to save the contract from invalidity. You will learn not only the ruling but also the subtle linguistic analysis that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a hire contract (ijara) for a slave for two months at two different rates (four and five dirhams). The ruling: the contract is valid (sahih), with the first month at the first-mentioned rate.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is a linguistic analysis: the indefinite 'shahran' (a month) would invalidate the contract due to uncertainty. The principle of taḥarrīyan li al-ṣiḥḥa requires interpreting the indefinite as the nearest month to preserve validity.
This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'a month at four' is indefinite. Indefiniteness invalidates the hire. To avoid that, the indefinite 'a month' is specified as the first month (closest to the contract). The second month then necessarily gets the other rate.
The second month is the only remaining distinct month.
4
Note the objection (ikhtilaf)
An objector argues that the reasoning assumes the month is indefinite, but the hirer's speech uses definite terms ('the two months'), so there is no indefiniteness. The objection questions the basis of the entire reasoning.
This is a methodological challenge.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi answer: The definite 'the two months' in the hirer's statement refers back to the owner's original indefinite mention ('two months'), so the months remain unspecified. Thus the original reasoning holds, and the contract stands.
This response relies on the distinction between the owner's and hirer's statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول بأربعة إلخ : يعني إذا آجر عبده لشهرين شهرا منهما بأربعة، وشهرا بخمسة صح العقد، ويكون الشهر الأول بأربعة والثاني بخمسة؛ لأنه لما قال أولا: 'شهرا بأربعة' انصرف إلى ما يلي العقد تحريا للصحة كما لو سكت عليه فقط؛ لأن تنكير الشهر مفسد للإجارة، فتعين فإذا انصرف الأول إلى ما يليه انصرف الثاني إلى الأخير تحريا؛ لأنه أقرب الأوقات إليه، فصار كما لو صرّح به. واعترض عليه بأن هذا التعليل مبني على أن يكون الشهر منكرا والمذكور ليس كذلك، فكيف يستقيم التعليل؟ وأجيب بأن المذكور قول المستأجر واللام فيه للعهد لما في كلام المؤجر من التنكير بأن قال المؤجر: أجرت منك هذا العبد شهرين شهرا بأربعة وشهرا بخمسة فقال المستأجر: استأجرت منك هذا العبد هذين الشهرين شهرا بأربعة وشهرا بخمسة، فاللام في الشهرين عهد لقول المؤجر شهرين منكرا فلا يكون الشهران متعينين، ولو قال المصنف: 'ولو قبل إجارة عبد إلى آخره لكان أولى فافهم واحفظ فتح وتكمله وغيرهما بتوضيح من المحشي)
And the first at four... meaning: If he hires out his slave for two months, one of them at four and one at five, the contract is valid, and the first month is at four and the second at five because when he said first 'a month at four', it refers to the month immediately following the contract, seeking validity, as if he only mentioned that. Because making the month indefinite invalidates the hire, so it becomes specified. When the first is referred to the closest, the second is referred to the last, seeking validity, because it is the nearest time to it, so it is as if he explicitly stated. Objection: This reasoning is based on the month being indefinite, but the mention is not so, how can the reasoning stand? Answer: The mentioned (definite) is the statement of the hirer, and the 'lam' is for reference to the indefinite in the owner's speech: the owner said 'two months, a month at four and a month at five' and the hirer said 'I hire these two months, a month at four and a month at five', so the 'lam' in 'the two months' refers to the owner's indefinite 'two months', so the two months are not definite. And if the author had said 'and if before hiring a slave etc.' it would have been better, so understand and memorize, Fath and Takmila and others with clarification from the marginal annotator.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling and proof on the validity of hiring a slave for two months with different rental rates: the first month at four dirhams and the second at five. The author explains that the contract is valid and the rates are assigned to the first and second months respectively. The reasoning is that when the owner says 'a month at four', it automatically refers to the month immediately following the contract, because making the month indefinite would invalidate the hire. Then the remaining distinct month must be the later one. An objection is raised: the reasoning assumes the month is indefinite (indefinite noun 'shahran'), but in the contract phrasing both parties use defined terms ('the two months'). The answer is that the definition in the hirer's speech refers back to the owner's indefinite mention, so the months are not truly definite. The author recommends studying Fath and Takmila for clarity. This shows the sophisticated Hanafi approach to contract interpretation to preserve validity (tahrīyan li al-ṣiḥḥa).

Key Terms
تنكير الشهر (tankīr al-shahr): The indefinite form of 'month' (shahran) which would make the hire invalid if not specified.
تحريا للصحة (taḥarrīyan li al-ṣiḥḥa): Seeking validity; a principle of interpretation that leans toward making the contract valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The second month.
B) The month immediately following the contract.
C) Either month equally.
D) The month chosen by the slave.

Key Takeaway
A hire contract with different rates for two months is valid; the first rate applies to the first month by default, following the principle of seeking validity.

Resolving Disputes over Hired Slave's Condition: Hukm al-Hal
Ruling on burden of proof when hirer and owner dispute the slave's condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school uses the current observable state (hukm al-hal) to resolve disputes between the hirer and owner of a hired slave regarding the slave's illness or running away.

Core Idea
When hirer and owner disagree about the slave's condition, the judge decides based on what is currently observable.

In the Hanafi school, not all disputes require witnesses or oaths. In certain Ijara cases, the court relies on the apparent, observable condition as decisive evidence. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that when the hirer and owner dispute whether the slave ran away or fell ill during the rental period, the judge looks at the present state to determine who is truthful, shifting the burden of proof accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
The hirer claims the slave ran away or fell ill during the rental period; the owner denies or claims it happened just before delivery.
2
Recognize the dispute scenario
There is no direct evidence—only conflicting statements from two parties.
3
Apply the principle of hukm al-hal
The judge observes the current condition of the slave. If the slave is currently ill or absent, the hirer's claim is supported; if healthy and present, the owner's statement is preferred.
4
Understand the legal effect
The burden of proof shifts to the party whose claim contradicts the current state. The party whose claim matches the current state is believed without further proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلفا في إباق العبد ومرضه حكم الحال، يعني لو استأجر عبدا شهرا مثلا، ثم قال المستأجر في آخر الشهر أبق العبد أو مرض في المدة، وأنكر المولى ذلك، أو أنكر استناده إلى أول المدة، فقال: أصابه المرض قبل أن تأتيني بساعة، يحكم الحال، فيكون القول -
And if they dispute regarding the slave's running away or illness, the ruling is based on the current state. Meaning: If he hires a slave for a month, and then at the end of the month the hirer says the slave ran away or fell ill during the period, and the owner denies that, or denies that it started from the beginning of the month, saying: the illness befell him an hour before he came to me, then the judge looks at the current state, so the word is [with the one who matches the current state].
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on how to resolve a dispute between the hirer and the owner of a hired slave regarding the slave's condition (running away or illness). When the hirer claims that the slave ran away or fell ill during the rental period (which would affect liability or obligation), and the owner denies or claims the illness was recent, the judge does not simply apply the normal rule of burden of proof. Instead, the judge looks at the present state (hukm al-hal): if the slave is currently ill or absent, the hirer's claim is supported, and the owner must prove otherwise; if the slave is healthy and present, the owner's word is taken. This is a qa'ida (legal maxim) that relies on observable evidence. The text is incomplete in the OCR, but the principle is clear: the court uses the current visible condition as circumstantial evidence to favor the party whose claim matches reality.

Key Terms
حكم الحال (ḥukm al-ḥāl): Ruling based on the current observable state of affairs; a type of circumstantial evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The judge believes the owner unless the hirer brings witnesses.
B) The judge looks at the current state of the slave (present or absent) to decide who is truthful.
C) The judge asks the slave himself.
D) The judge cancels the contract.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over a hired slave's condition, the Hanafi school decides based on the current observable state, not on a fixed burden of proof.

Rulings on Hiring a Slave and His Receipt of Wages
Rulings on slave hiring himself, owner's right to wage, and validity of slave's receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a slave's receipt of wages, explain the owner's right to take the wage, and apply the rule of indefinite terms to determine hire rates.

Core Idea
A slave's receipt of wages is valid and discharges the hirer; the owner may take the wage as his property.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary addresses a key issue in ijara: when a slave hires himself out, does his receipt of wages have legal effect? The Hanafi school affirms its validity by istihsan, and also addresses how to determine rates when the hire period is ambiguously defined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
The passage covers three distinct rulings: (1) the owner's right to take the wage the slave earns, (2) the validity of the slave's receipt of that wage, and (3) a case of hiring a slave for two months with different rates. Locate each in the text.
The rulings are interconnected: the owner's right is established first, then the slave's receipt is validated, and finally a practical scenario is resolved.
2
Follow the owner's right argument
The author argues that the owner may take the wage from the slave because it is the owner's property. This is supported by analogies: like stolen goods in a thief's hand (the thief is liable but the slave is not), and because the wage is a substitute for the benefit, which the owner owns.
The key point: the slave does not own the wage; it belongs to the master.
3
Understand the istihsan for slave's receipt
The slave's receipt of wages is valid by istihsan ( juristic preference). The text explains it is a 'pure benefit' (naf' mahd) and the slave is the direct party to the contract (mubashir al-'aqd). This is analogous to accepting a gift, which a slave can do with permission.
Consensus is cited: 'by consensus' (bil-ijma').
4
Apply the rule of indefinite terms
When a slave is hired for two months with two different rates mentioned as 'a month for four and a month for five', the first month is assigned the first rate. This is to preserve validity (taharrin li al-sihha), because indefinite terms would void the contract.
The reasoning responds to an objection about indefinite wording; the reply clarifies that the hirer's repetition makes the months definite.
5
Note the objections and answers
An objection is raised that the reasoning depends on the month being indefinite, but it is actually definite. The answer is that the indefiniteness is in the owner's initial speech, while the hirer's speech repeats it with a definite article (al-), thus the months become specified.
This shows the meticulous nature of Hanafi textual analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إجارة العبد من أجره، ولو وجده ربه أخذه، وصح قبض العبد أجره، ولو آجر عبده هذين الشهرين شهرا بأربعة، وشهرا بخمسة صح، والأول بأربعة. الحاصل من إيجاره بنفسه رجل - فصار نظير المال المسروق في يد السارق بعد القطع، ولأن الأجرة بدل المنفعة، والبدل حكمه حكم المبدل، ولو أتلف الغاصب المنفعة لا يضمن فكذا بدلها وما تردد بين أصلين توفر حظهما، فرجحنا جانب المالك عند بقاء الأجر في يده، فقلنا: المالك أحق به، ورجحنا جانب الغاصب في حق الضمان وقلنا لا ضمان عليه إذا أكل الأجرة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٦/٨ أخذه أي ما في يد العبد من الأجر لأنه عين ماله ولا يلزم من بطلان التقوم بطلان الملك رمز الحقائق: ٢٨٥/٢ وصح قبض العبد إلخ الأنه نفع محض مأذون فيه كقبول الهبة يعني إذا آجر العبد نفسه وأعطاه المستأجر صح قبضه بالإجماع؛ لأنه المباشر للعقد، وحقوق العقد إليه، فيصح؛ لكونه مأذونا في التصرف النافع. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٧/٨ وفائدة صحة القبض تظهر في حق خروج المستأجر عن عهدة الأجرة، فإنه يحصل الخروج عن العهدة بالأداء إلى العبد بخلاف ما لو آجره المولى، فإنه لا القبض إلا بوكالة المولى؛ لأنه العاقد. (فتح، تكملة) والأول بأربعة إلخ : يعني إذا آجر عبده لشهرين شهرا منهما بأربعة، وشهرا بخمسة صح العقد، ويكون الشهر الأول بأربعة والثاني بخمسة؛ لأنه لما قال أولا: "شهرا بأربعة" انصرف إلى ما يلي العقد تحريا للصحة كما لو سكت عليه فقط؛ لأن تنكير الشهر مفسد للإجارة، فتعين فإذا انصرف الأول إلى ما يليه انصرف الثاني إلى الأخير تحريا؛ لأنه أقرب الأوقات إليه، فصار كما لو صرّح به. واعترض عليه بأن هذا التعليل مبني على أن يكون الشهر منكرا والمذكور ليس كذلك، فكيف يستقيم التعليل؟ وأجيب بأن المذكور قول المستأجر واللام فيه للعهد لما في كلام المؤجر من التنكير بأن قال المؤجر: أجرت منك هذا العبد شهرين شهرا بأربعة وشهرا بخمسة فقال المستأجر: استأجرت منك هذا العبد هذين الشهرين شهرا بأربعة وشهرا بخمسة، فاللام في الشهرين عهد لقول المؤجر شهرين منكرا فلا يكون الشهران متعينين، ولو قال المصنف: "ولو قبل إجارة عبد إلى آخره لكان أولى فافهم واحفظ فتح وتكمله وغيرهما بتوضيح من المحشي)
The book of hiring. Hiring of the slave. Whoever hires him, and if his owner finds him, he takes him, and the slave's receipt of his wage is valid. If he hires his slave for these two months, a month for four and a month for five, it is valid, and the first is for four. The profit from his hiring himself is like the stolen property in the hand of the thief after amputation, and because the wage is the substitute for the benefit, and the substitute has the same ruling as the substituted. If the usurper destroys the benefit, he is not liable, so likewise its substitute. When it fluctuates between two origins, each takes its share, so we favored the owner's side when the wage remains in his hand, so we said: the owner has more right to it, and we favored the usurper's side regarding liability and said there is no liability on him if he consumes the wage. Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/66: He takes what is in the slave's hand of the wage because it is his property, and the invalidity of valuation does not necessitate the invalidity of ownership. Symbol of Truths: 2/285: The slave's receipt of the wage is valid etc. because it is a pure benefit permitted, like accepting a gift. That is, if the slave hires himself and the hirer gives him the wage, his receipt is valid by consensus; because he is the direct party to the contract, and the rights of the contract belong to him, so it is valid because he is permitted in beneficial disposition. Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/67: The benefit of the validity of receipt appears in terms of the hirer being discharged from the liability for the wage, for he is discharged by payment to the slave, unlike if the master hired him out, for he does not receive except by the master's agency, because he is the contractor. (Fath, Takmilah) 'And the first is for four' etc.: That is, if he hires his slave for two months, one month for four and one month for five, the contract is valid, and the first month is for four and the second for five; because when he said first: "a month for four" it refers to what is adjacent to the contract, seeking validity, as if he merely remained silent on it; because the indefiniteness of the month invalidates the hire, so it is specified. When the first refers to the adjacent, the second refers to the last, seeking validity; because it is the closest times to it, so it is as if he explicitly stated it. It was objected that this reasoning is based on the month being indefinite, but the mentioned is not such, so how can the reasoning stand? And it was answered that the mentioned is the speech of the hirer, and the lam is for the covenant, due to the indefiniteness in the owner's speech: the owner said: "I hired to you this slave for two months, a month for four and a month for five" and the hirer said: "I hired from you this slave for these two months, a month for four and a month for five" so the lam in 'two months' is a covenant for the owner's speech 'two months' which is indefinite, so the two months are not specified. 'And if the author had said: "Or before hiring a slave..." it would have been better, so understand and memorize Fath, Takmilah, and others with clarification from the commentator.)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and commentary on the hiring of a slave (ijarat al-'abd). It covers three main points: (1) the owner's right to take the wage earned by his slave from self-hiring; (2) the validity of the slave's own receipt of that wage; and (3) a specific case where a slave is hired for two months at different rates. The author explains that the owner may take the wage from the slave because it is the owner's property, analogous to stolen goods in a thief's hand after amputation—though the thief is liable for the goods, the slave is not liable because he is merely property. The slave's receipt of the wage is valid by consensus because he is the direct party in the contract of hire; the benefit of this validity is that the hirer is discharged from liability upon paying the slave. In the two-month hire case, if the contract is ambiguously worded, the first month is assigned the first rate to preserve validity, as indefinite terms would otherwise void the contract. Objections and answers clarify the reasoning.

Key Terms
قبض العبد (qabd al-'abd): The physical receipt of the wage by the slave from the hirer.
إجارة العبد (ijarat al-'abd): Hiring out of a slave, either by the master or by the slave himself with permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave's receipt of wages from self-hiring valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the slave is the owner of the wage.
B) Because the slave is the direct party to the contract and it is a pure beneficial act.
C) Because the master has explicitly authorized the slave to receive wages.
D) Because the wage is a substitute for the benefit and follows the ruling of the benefit.

Key Takeaway
The slave's receipt of wages is valid per istihsan, and the owner's right to take the wage is absolute; ambiguous hire terms are resolved in favor of validity.

Dispute over Slave's Condition during Hire: Hukm al-Hal
Ruling on disputes between hirer and owner about slave's condition during hire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of hukm al-hal to resolve disputes where the current condition indicates a past state, including analogizing from the mill water example.

Core Idea
Current state determines burden of oath: the party supported by the apparent condition has their statement accepted.

When a hired slave is allegedly ill or absent, and the hirer and owner dispute the timing, which party should be believed? The Hanafi school employs hukm al-hal: the current apparent condition is used as a preponderating factor. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, justification, and analogical extension from a famous mill water case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize the conflicting claims: the hirer asserts that the slave absconded or fell ill during the rental period, while the owner denies or says it happened after the period.
This is a procedural dispute about the timing of a known event.
2
Determine the current state
Observe the slave's condition at the time of litigation: is he absent (still missing) or ill (if recovered, the state may have changed)? The current state is the starting point.
The principle assumes continuity: what is now was probably true in the recent past.
3
Apply hukm al-hal
The party whose claim aligns with the current state (e.g., slave is currently ill, so hirer's claim is supported) is given the 'saying' (al-qawl) – their statement is accepted upon taking an oath.
The other party may also swear, but the burden shifts.
4
Understand the legal basis
The justification: 'its existence in the present indicates its existence in the past' (وجوده في الحال يدل على وجوده في الماضي). This is a form of istishab (presumption of continuity) acting as a preponderator (murajjih), not conclusive proof.
The text explicitly states it is a preponderator, not a proof (حجة).
5
Apply the analogy to mill water
The same reasoning applies to a dispute about a mill's water flow: if the mill is currently dry, the statement of the one claiming it was dry during the rental is accepted, because the current state indicates the past.
This analogy confirms the method is systematic across disputes involving ongoing conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلفا في إباق العبد ومرضه حكم الحال، والقول لرب الثوب في القميص والقباء والحمرة والصفرة والأجر وعدمه. يعني لو استأجر عبدا شهرا مثلا، ثم قال المستأجر في آخر الشهر أبق العبد أو مرض في المدة، وأنكر المولى ذلك، أو أنكر استناده إلى أول المدة، فقال: أصابه المرض قبل أن تأتيني بساعة، يحكم الحال، فيكون القول - قول من شهد له الحال مع يمينه؛ لأن القول في الدعاوي قول من يشهد له الظاهر، ووجوده في الحال يدل على وجوده في الماضي، فيصلح الظاهر مرجحا وإن لم يصلح حجة كما إذا اختلفا في جريان ماء الطاحون وانقطاعه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٦٧/٨
And if they dispute regarding the absconding of the slave and his illness, the rule is the current state. And the saying is with the owner of the cloth regarding the shirt and the cloak and the red and the yellow and the wage and its absence. That is, if someone hires a slave for a month, for example, then the hirer says at the end of the month: the slave absconded or fell ill during the period, and the owner denies that, or denies its attribution to the beginning of the period, saying: the illness struck him an hour before you brought him to me, the current state is judged, so the saying is for the one whom the current state supports, with his oath; because the saying in claims is for the one whom the apparent supports, and its existence at present indicates its existence in the past, so the apparent is suitable as a preponderator even if not suitable as a proof, like if they dispute about the flow of mill water and its cessation. Completion of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/67
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the hirer and the owner of a slave regarding whether the slave absconded or fell ill during the hire period. The Hanafi principle applied here is 'hukm al-hal'—judging by the current state. The rule is that the person whose claim is supported by the apparent state of affairs at the time of dispute (e.g., the slave is presently healthy or present) has the burden of proof shifted to them? Actually, the text says 'al-qawl li man shahida lahu al-hal'—the saying is for the one whom the current state supports, with his oath. This means that if the slave is currently sick, the hirer's claim that he was sick during the term is given weight, and the owner must take an oath if he denies. The justification is that the current existence of a condition (e.g., illness) is evidence that it existed in the past, because conditions tend to persist. This is analogous to a dispute about a mill's water flow: if the mill is currently dry, the person claiming it was dry during the rental period is believed. This is not a definitive proof but serves as a preponderating factor (murajjih) in the absence of other evidence.

Key Terms
حكم الحال (hukm al-hal): The legal ruling based on the current apparent state of a person or thing at the time of dispute.
إباق (ibaq): Absconding or running away of a slave.
مرجح (murajjih): A preponderating factor that gives weight to one claim over another, but is not conclusive proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hired slave is currently healthy, but the hirer claims he was sick during the rental term, whose statement is accepted with oath?
A) The owner's, because health supports his denial.
B) The hirer's, because the hirer is the plaintiff.
C) Both must jointly swear.
D) The judge appoints a random oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when the current condition supports one party's claim in a dispute about a past event, that party's statement is accepted with oath due to the principle of hukm al-hal.
