Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute commentary on the rescission of ijarah contracts, presenting the Hanafi positions and responding to Shafi'i objections. The text begins by discussing three controversial issues among early scholars (mashayikh) regarding the cessation of water supply to leased property. Some held that the contract automatically rescinds, while others required an explicit rescission; the author favors the latter because the usufruct may potentially return, similar to a slave absconding before delivery. The main ruling states that ijarah is rescinded by death of a party if the contract was made for oneself, but not if made for another (e.g., agent, executor, mutawalli). The Hanafi proof is that the usufruct and rent become estate property, and the heirs are not party to the original contract. However, if necessity arises (e.g., death en route to Mecca), the contract remains and the judge acts in the best interest. The passage then addresses agency: if the agent dies, the contract is not rescinded because the entitlement remains, but the one for whom it was contracted is considered the real party. An intra-Hanafi difference appears regarding whether the agent for hiring is deemed a buyer for himself; the preferred view is that the contract is not invalidated by the agent's death. The text then moves to rescission by option of condition (khiyar al-shart) and option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah). Hanafis permit both based on the principle that ijarah is a commutative contract analogous to sale, while Shafi'is object due to impossibility of returning usufruct during the option period and ignorance of the subject matter. The Hanafi response relies on the permissibility of adding ijarah to future time and the hadith of Ali about option of sight. Finally, the passage discusses rescission by excuse ('udhr), noting that it requires a court order according to most scholars, though some hold it occurs automatically. The commentary emphasizes the Hanafi reliance on qiyas and istihsan, and the practical differences with the Shafi'i school.

Key Terms
فسخ الإجارة (faskh al-ijarah): Legal termination of an ijarah contract before its expiry due to death or other causes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a lessor dies during an ijarah contract that he made for himself, what happens to the contract?
A) The contract continues automatically and the heirs collect rent.
B) The contract is rescinded automatically without need for formal rescission.
C) The contract is rescinded only by court order.
D) The contract becomes voidable at the heir's option.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, death of a party to an ijarah contract made for oneself automatically rescinds the contract because usufructs are consumed and consent cannot continue.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
8 min
Termination of Ijarah by Option of Condition and Sight: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on termination of ijarah by options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on termination of ijarah by khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using qiyas and dalil.

Core Idea
Ijarah terminates by khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah like sale, despite Shafi'i objections.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute: can an ijarah contract be terminated by the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) or the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah)? The Hanafi school, reasoning by analogy to sale, allows both. The Shafi'i school objects based on the unique nature of usufruct. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school extends contractual options beyond sales to hire and lease contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school permits termination of ijarah by both khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah. This applies to lessor and lessee alike for khiyar al-shart, and to the lessee only for khiyar al-ru'yah.
The text explicitly says 'tufsakh bi-khiyar al-shart wa al-ru'yah'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its basis
Al-Shafi'i argues that khiyar al-shart is invalid because usufruct cannot be fully returned during the option period; for khiyar al-ru'yah, ijarah is a purchase of benefits that must be known to avoid gharar.
The passage summarizes the Shafi'i dalil: for khiyar al-shart, the lessee cannot return the full usufruct; for khiyar al-ru'yah, buying unseen benefits involves ignorance.
3
Trace the Hanafi qiyas to sale
The Hanafi response is that ijarah is a commutative contract (aqd mu'awada) like sale. Just as options are valid in sale by explicit permission or analogy, they are valid in ijarah. The hadith of Ali (r.a.) on khiyar al-ru'yah in sale extends to benefits.
The text states: 'Li-annahu aqd mu'awada... fa-yajuz shart al-khiyar fihi ka al-bay'.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on qada/rida
A later Hanafi dispute: does termination require qada (court decree) or rida (consent)? The relied-upon view (Ibn Abidin) holds that for khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah, no qada or rida is required, unlike khiyar al-ayb in total destruction cases.
This is a refinement within the school; the text cites al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, in Hanafi law, parties can stipulate a three-day option in a lease, and a lessee can reject an unseen property upon sight. In Shafi'i law, such stipulations are invalid, and property must be seen before contracting.
The khilaf has direct impact on lease contracts and real estate rentals.

(فتح بقدر الحاجة) تفسخ الخ: لما فرغ عن بيان الفسخ بالعيب شرع في بيان الفسخ بخيار الشرط والرؤية وغيرهما، يعني إذا شرط المؤجر أو المستأجر خيار الشرط، أو كل منهما خيار الشرط ثلاثة أيام، فله أن يفسخ الإجارة به عندنا، وكذا إذا استأجر شيئا لم يره، فإذا رآه له أن لا يقبله ويفسخ الإجارة، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز الفسخ بهما، أما في الأول؛ فلأن المستأجر لا يمكنه رد المعقود عليه بكماله إن كان الخيار له؛ إذ لابد من تلف شيء في مدة الخيار، وإن كان للمؤجر فلا يمكنه التسليم أيضا على الكمال؛ لأن ما مضى من المنفعة مدة الخيار لا يمكن تسليمه، وأما عدم الجواز في الثاني؛ فلأن الإجارة شراء المنافع، فلا يجوز أن يشتري ما لم يره للجهالة. ولنا في الأول: أنه عقد معاوضة، ولا يجب قبضه في المجلس، ويحتمل الفسخ بالإقالة، فيجوز شرط الخيار فيه كالبيع، قال في "العيني": وهذا أي الاختلاف في خيار الشرط مبني على أن إضافة الإجارة إلى الزمان المستقبل كما إذا أضافها إلى شهر رمضان، وهو في شعبان يجوز عندنا، فيصح اشتراط الخيار، ولا يجوز عنده، فلا الخيار. ولنا في الثاني: أن هذه الجهالة لا تمنع الجواز؛ لأنها لا تفضي إلى المنازعة؛ لأنه إذا رآه و لم يوافقه رده، فإذا رآه ثبت له خيار فسخ؛ لأن العقد لا يتم إلا بالرضاء، ولا رضا بدون العلم، وقال علي: "من اشترى شيئا لم يره فله الخيار إذا رآه "، ولأن الإجارة شراء المنافع، فتناولها الحديث. واعلم أنه اختلف في أنه يشترط في الفسخ بخيار الشرط وخيار الرؤية الرضاء أو القضاء كما يشترط في خيار العيب أم لا؟ وظاهر ما في الدر المختار: أنه يشترط فيهما أيضا، قال العلامة ابن عابدين في رد المحتار" بعد ما بحث فيه: والحاصل أنه لا يشترط القضاء أو الرضاء في خيار الشرط والرؤية، وأما في خيار العيب، ففي نحو انهدام الدار كلها يفسخ بغيبة صاحبه بخلاف انهدام الجدار ونحوه كما مر انتهى بقدر الحاجة، ثم اعلم أن خيار الرؤية يثبت للمستأجر، وأما للمؤجر فلا، قال في "رد المحتار: وأما خيار الرؤية فلا يكون للمؤجر كما في البيع.
Book of Ijarah / Termination of Ijarah / And the executor and trustee in the waqf, and it is terminated by option of condition, option of sight, and by excuse according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. / It is terminated. / He said in al-Fath... [commentary]. / Know that there is disagreement about whether termination by khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah requires consent or court decree... Then know that khiyar al-ru'yah is established for the lessee, not for the lessor.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. It presents the Hanafi position that an ijarah contract can be terminated by khiyar al-shart (option of condition) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight), contrary to al-Shafi'i who disallows both. The commentary explains the Shafi'i objections: for khiyar al-shart, that the subject matter (usufruct) cannot be fully returned; for khiyar al-ru'yah, that ijarah is a purchase of benefits and must be seen to avoid ignorance. The Hanafi response relies on analogy to sale (qiyas): ijarah is a commutative contract, so options are valid. The passage also resolves an intra-Hanafi issue about whether termination requires qada or rida: in khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah, it does not; only in khiyar al-ayb (defect) does it require in cases like total collapse. Finally, it notes that khiyar al-ru'yah only benefits the lessee, not the lessor, as in sale.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in the contract allowing either party to rescind within a specified period (usually three days).
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option to rescind a contract upon seeing the subject matter if it was unseen at the time of contract.
إجارة (ijarah): A contract for hire or lease, where the lessor provides a benefit (usufruct) in exchange for rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for allowing khiyar al-shart in ijarah?
A) Ijma' of the Companions
B) Qiyas to sale because ijarah is a commutative contract
C) A direct Quranic verse
D) Istihsan due to necessity

Key Takeaway
Ijarah is analogous to sale in Hanafi law, so khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah are valid termination options.

Termination of Ijarah by Excuse: Concept and Examples
Definition and examples of excuses terminating ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a valid excuse in Hanafi ijarah, apply it to the four classic examples, and explain why the Hanafi school permits termination by excuses beyond defects.

Core Idea
An excuse is a hardship not assumed; termination requires active faskh, but apparent excuses need no court decree.

This lesson examines the Hanafi doctrine of terminating ijarah by excuse. The passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq defines 'udhr as a circumstance that makes continuation excessively burdensome, and then gives concrete examples. You will learn the legal criteria for a valid excuse and see how the Hanafi school balances contractual bindingness with preventing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'udhr
Read the definition: an inability to continue the contract without bearing extra harm not justified by the contract. This sets the threshold for a valid excuse.
Contrast with Shafi'i: they only accept defects, not external excuses.
2
Analyze the four examples
Work through each example (tooth extraction, wedding feast, shop bankruptcy, lessor's debt) and identify the extra harm that would occur if the contract continued.
In each case, the harm is not part of the original agreement.
3
Identify the requirement of active faskh
Note that the majority of Hanafi scholars require an act of termination (faskh) rather than automatic dissolution. The passage reconciles the reports from al-Ziyadat versus al-Jami' and al-Asl.
Automatic termination would cause uncertainty; active faskh gives clarity.
4
Distinguish apparent vs. hidden excuses
Qadi Khan and al-Mahbubi: if the excuse is apparent (e.g., imprisonment), no court decree is needed; if hidden (e.g., debt not widely known), a decree is required to prove it.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab difference
Compare with al-Shafi'i's view that only defects excuse termination. The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: a bankrupt lessee can rescind in Hanafi law but not in Shafi'i.
The Hanafi reliance on istihsan (juristic preference) justifies this broader scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(تكملة، فتح، ملا مسكين)
استأجر إلخ بيان لأمثلة الأعذار التي تفسخ به الإجارات يعني إذا استأجر رجل رجلا ليقلع ضرسه، فسكن الوجع قبل أن يقلع، له أن يفسخ الإجارة؛ لأنه لو بقي العقد لزم قلع سن صحيح، وهو غير مستحق بالعقد، فهذا عذر يمنع المضي في موجب العقد إلخ، وكذا إذا استأجر طباخا ليطبخ طعام الوليمة، فاختلعت المرأة قبل أن يطبخ له فسخ الإجارة؛ لأنه لو بقي العقد يتضرر المستأجر بإتلاف ماله في غير الوليمة، وهو غير مستحق بالعقد، وهذا أيضا عذر يمنع المضي في موجب العقد إلخ، وكذا إذا استأجر حانوتا ليتجر فيه، فأفلس المستأجر قبل أن يتجر فيه له فسخ الإجارة؛ لأنه أيضا عذر يمنع المضي في موجب العقد. (بفتح بزيادة) فاختلعت: أو ماتت المرأة قبل الزفاف فأفلس إلخ إما بديون كثيرة، وصار بحال لا تأمنه الناس على أمتعتهم، أو بظهور خيانته عند الناس، فامتنعوا عن معاملته. (فتح) أو آجره ولزمه إلخ: يعني إذا آجر حانوته، ولزمه دين ثبت بمشاهدة من الناس، أو بإقامة البينة عليه، أو بإقرار منه، ولا مال له سواه، ولا يقدر على قضائه إلا ببيع ما آجر، يفسخ الإجارة في هذه الحالة؛ لأنه لو بقي العقد لزم ضرر الحبس بأن يحبس القاضي المؤجر في الدين، ويكفي توقع الحبس وإن لم يحبس بالفعل على ما يظهر من كلام "الهداية"، ويخالفه ما في "المبسوط" حيث قال : إذا كان على المؤجر دين فحبس فهو عذر (حموي عن البرجندي) وأقول في كون كلام "المبسوط": يفيد عدم الاكتفاء بتوقع الحبس، وأنه لابد من الحبس بالفعل نظر ظاهر؛ لأن التنصيص على الشيء لا ينفي الحكم عما عداه واختلفوا في كيفية الفسخ قال بعضهم: يبيع الدار أولا، فينفذ بيعه، وتنفسخ الإجارة ضمنا، وقال بعضهم: يفسخ الإجارة أولا ثم يبيع. (فتح بزيادة) بعيان أو ببيان إلخ والظاهر أن أحدهما يضمن عن الآخر يعني قوله بعيان أو ببيان من المؤجر؛ لأن العيان البينة التي يقيمها رب الدين والبيان البينة التي يقيمها المؤجر على ما ركبه من الدين، أقول: ذكر العيني وغيره: أن العيان المشاهدة. من الناس، والبيان إقامة البيئة، فيكون عطف البيان على العيان من عطف المغاير، ولا يستغنى حينئذ بأحدهما عن الآخر. (فتح باختصار)
And by excuse etc. [And al-Shafi'i said: they are not terminated by excuses except defect]. It means that ijarah is terminated by an excuse which is inability to proceed with the contract except by bearing additional harm not justified by the contract. Then when the excuse is verified, it is terminated by the excuse itself according to some scholars, but according to most scholars it requires termination, which is correct... / The excuse is the inability of the contracting party to proceed with the contract except by bearing additional harm not justified... / Such as if a person hires a man to extract his tooth and the pain subsides, or to cook for a wedding feast and the woman repudiates, or hires a shop to trade and becomes bankrupt, or he leases and a debt becomes incumbent on him, whether witnessed or manifest. / Does it require court decree or consent of the other party? In al-Ziyadat: it requires; in al-Jami' and al-Asl: it does not; Qadi Khan and al-Mahbubi reconciled: if the excuse is apparent, no decree; if hidden, decree is needed to prove it. / Examples: tooth extraction, cooking, shop, debt of lessor. / Regarding 'bi'aynin aw bi bayan': the apparent meaning is that one includes the other; 'ayn is the evidence from the creditor, bayan is the evidence from the lessor.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies termination of ijarah by excuse ('udhr). An excuse is a circumstance that makes it impossible to continue the contract without incurring excessive hardship not envisaged in the contract. The passage notes a disagreement among Hanafi scholars: some say the contract terminates automatically upon the excuse, but the majority (and the relied-upon view) hold that termination requires a specific act of faskh. It then gives classic examples: (1) hiring a dentist to extract a tooth, then the pain stops—the lessee may rescind because pulling a healthy tooth is not owed; (2) hiring a cook for a wedding feast, then the bride cancels the marriage—the lessee would waste money; (3) hiring a shop for trade, then the lessee becomes bankrupt—he cannot trade; (4) a lessor incurs such debt that the court may imprison him, forcing sale of the leased property. In the last case, the passage discusses whether mere fear of imprisonment suffices (as in al-Hidayah) or actual imprisonment is needed (as in al-Mabsut). It also resolves that the lessor's debt must be proven by clear evidence ('iyan or bayan). Finally, it addresses the procedure: some say sell the property first (which impliedly terminates the lease), others say terminate first then sell. The passage also covers the condition for termination: if the excuse is apparent (like imprisonment), no court decree is needed; if hidden, the court must verify it.

Key Terms
العذر (al-'udhr): A legitimate excuse that prevents fulfillment of the contract without bearing excessive harm not justified by the contract.
فسخ (faskh): The act of rescinding or terminating a contract, either by one party or by court order.
عيان ('iyan): Visible evidence; here, testimony of witnesses that the debt is due.
بيان (bayan): Clarification; here, evidence presented by the lessor to prove the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does an excuse ('udhr) require a court decree for termination?
A) Always, regardless of the excuse
B) Only when the excuse is hidden and not obvious
C) Never, the contract terminates automatically
D) Only when the lessor is involved

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law permits termination of ijarah by excuse to prevent hardship, but requires active faskh; the need for a court decree depends on how apparent the excuse is.

Rescission of Hire: Valid Excuses and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Qada Condition
Defines faskh, lists excuses, and discusses qada requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid excuses for rescinding a hire contract and apply the reconciled Hanafi rule on whether a court order (qada) is required.

Core Idea
Valid excuses allow rescission without qada if apparent; hidden excuses require qada to prevent disputes.

Rescission of a hire contract (faskh al-ijarah) is permitted when the contract's continuation would cause additional harm not originally entitled. But does every valid excuse give the right to rescind without judicial oversight? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether a court order (qada) or the other party's consent is needed. This lesson walks through the definition, examples, and the reconciled position that distinguishes between apparent and hidden excuses.

Guided Path
1
Define faskh and the criterion of additional harm
Understand the legal definition: faskh is the inability to proceed without bearing harm beyond what was agreed.
This is the threshold for a valid excuse.
2
Identify the examples of valid excuses in the text
Recognize the scenarios: tooth pain subsiding, wedding cancellation, bankruptcy, lessor debt, and change of travel plan.
Each example illustrates a different type of harm.
3
Contrast the two Hanafi positions on the need for qada
Compare al-Ziyadat (requires qada/consent) with al-Asl and al-Jami' (no requirement).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the reconciliation rule: apparent vs. hidden excuses
Use the mufta bihi view: apparent excuses need no qada; hidden excuses require qada to verify.
The key is whether the excuse is obvious to others.
5
Note special cases: lessor debt and beast hire
Consider the nuances: imprisonment expectation vs actual imprisonment, and the hirer vs owner change of plan.
These illustrate how the rule is applied in practice.

(فتح باختصار)
أو بإقرار ولا مال له سواه أو استأجر دابة للسفر فبدا له منه لا للمكاري.
من المؤجر
للمؤجر الحانوت
أو بإقرار إلخ الظاهر أن المراد بالإقرار السابق على الإجارة وإلا يلزم أن يكون حجة متعدية. (ملا مسكين) أو استأجر إلخ: يعني لو استأجر دابة يسافر عليها، ثم بدا له أن لا يسافر، فهو عذر يفسخ به الإجارة، ولو بدا للمكاري لا يعذر؛ لأن المستأجر يلزمه ضرر ومشقة، وربما يفوته ما قصد كالحج وطلب الغريم، والمكاري لا يلزمه ذلك الضرر؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يقعد ويرسل غيره، وكذا لو مرض؛ لما ذكرنا، وروى الكرخي أنه عذر في حق المكاري؛ لأنه لا يعرو عن ضرر؛ ولأن غيره لا يشفق على دوابه مثله
Book of Hiring. Rescission of Hire. It is the inability of the contracting party to proceed with its obligation except by bearing additional harm not entitled by the contract. For example, the hirer for that by contract hired a man to extract his tooth, then the pain subsided; or to cook a wedding feast for him, then the woman divorced and he was no longer in need of extraction; or a shop to trade in, then he became bankrupt; or he rented out and a debt became binding on him by clear evidence or by declaration. (That is, from the man; that is, the hirer became poor; and it is the wedding feast; the woman.) Does it require a court order or consent of the other party or not? Mentioned in al-Ziyadat: that a court order or consent is required, and al-Jami' indicated and in al-Asl: that it is not required. And Qadi Khan and al-Mahbubi corrected the opinion of one who reconciled, saying: if the excuse is apparent, a court order is not needed because the excuse is apparent; and if it is not apparent, a court order is needed to make the excuse evident. (Takmila, Fath, Mulla Miskeen.) 'He hired etc.' is an explanation of examples of excuses by which hires are rescinded. Meaning if a man hired a man to extract his tooth, and the pain subsided before extraction, he may rescind the hire; because if the contract remained, it would necessitate extracting a healthy tooth, which is not entitled by the contract. This is an excuse preventing proceeding with the contract's obligation etc. Similarly, if he hired a cook to cook wedding food, and the woman divorced before he cooked for him, he rescinds the hire; because if the contract remained, the hirer would be harmed by wasting his wealth on other than the wedding, not entitled by the contract. This is also an excuse preventing proceeding etc. Similarly, if he hired a shop to trade in, and the hirer became bankrupt before trading in it, he may rescind the hire; because it is also an excuse preventing proceeding. (With Fath, with addition.) 'She divorced': or the woman died before the wedding, 'he became bankrupt' etc., either due to many debts, or he reached a state where people do not trust him with their goods, or due to his betrayal becoming known among people, so they refrained from dealing with him. (Fath) 'Or he rented out and it became binding on him etc.': meaning if he rented out his shop and a debt became binding on him established by witness' direct observation, or by legal evidence brought against him, or by his own acknowledgment, and he has no property besides it, and cannot pay it except by selling what he rented, he rescinds the hire in this case; because if the contract remained, harm of imprisonment would be necessary, in that the judge would imprison the lessor for the debt, and the expectation of imprisonment is sufficient even if not actually imprisoned, according to what appears from the words of al-Hidayah. And what is in al-Mabsut contradicts it, where it said: if the lessor has a debt and is imprisoned, it is an excuse. (Hamawi from al-Birjandi) And I say: in considering the words of al-Mabsut to indicate insufficiency of expecting imprisonment and that actual imprisonment is necessary, there is apparent consideration; because specifying something does not negate the ruling from what other than it. And they differed on the manner of rescission: some said he sells the house first, then his sale is executed and the hire is rescinded implicitly; others said he rescinds the hire first then sells. (Fath with addition) 'By clear evidence or by declaration etc.' And the apparent is that one of them is sufficient without the other? Meaning his statement 'by clear evidence or by declaration' from the lessor; because 'clear evidence' (ayan) is the evidence presented by the debt-owner, and 'declaration' (bayan) is the evidence presented by the lessor regarding the debt he incurred. I say: al-Ayni and others mentioned that 'ayan' is direct observation by people, and 'bayan' is presentation of evidence. So the conjunction of 'bayan' to 'ayan' is a conjunction of different things, and then one does not suffice without the other. (Fath abridged.) Or by acknowledgment and he has no property besides it, or he hired a beast for travel and then it appeared to him not to travel, not to the animal-owner. (From the lessor; for the lessor of the shop.) 'Or by acknowledgment etc.': The apparent is that what is meant by acknowledgment is that which precedes the hire; otherwise it would be a proof that applies to others. (Mulla Miskeen.) 'Or he hired etc.': meaning if he hired a beast to travel on, then it appeared to him not to travel, it is an excuse by which he rescinds the hire. And if it appeared to the animal-owner, it is not an excuse; because the hirer would suffer harm and hardship, and perhaps what he intended would be lost, like hajj and pursuing a creditor, and the animal-owner does not suffer that harm because he can sit and send someone else. Similarly, if he becomes ill, for what we mentioned. And al-Karkhi narrated that it is an excuse regarding the animal-owner, because it is not free from harm, and because someone else will not care for his beasts like he does.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage opens the section on rescission of hire contracts (faskh al-ijarah) in the Hanafi madhhab. It defines faskh as the inability of a contracting party to proceed with the contract's obligation except by bearing an additional harm that was not justified by the contract itself. This definition sets the criterion: a valid excuse must make continuation harmful beyond what was agreed. The text then lists several examples to illustrate what counts as a valid excuse. First: a man hires someone to extract a tooth, but the pain subsides before extraction. Since the purpose was pain relief, extracting a healthy tooth would be a harm not entitled by the contract, so the hirer may rescind. Second: a man hires a cook to prepare a wedding feast, but the bride divorces him before the cooking. Continuing would mean wasting food for no wedding, so rescission is allowed. Third: a man hires a shop for trade but becomes bankrupt (or is discovered to be dishonest) before trading. The bankruptcy makes the purpose impossible, so rescission is permitted. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: Does the right to rescind require a court order (qada) or the consent of the other party? According to al-Ziyadat, court order or consent is required. According to al-Jami' and al-Asl, it is not. Qadi Khan and al-Mahbubi reconciled: if the excuse is apparent (like the tooth pain subsiding or the bride divorcing), no court order is needed because the excuse is obvious. If the excuse is hidden (like bankruptcy or debt), a court order is needed to verify and make it known. The text then discusses the case of a lessor who incurs a debt and has no property but the rented shop. If the debt is established by witnesses' testimony, legal evidence, or acknowledgment, and the only way to pay is to sell the shop, the lessor may rescind the hire because otherwise he would face imprisonment. There is a difference of opinion in al-Hidayah (which says expectation of imprisonment suffices) and al-Mabsut (which requires actual imprisonment). The passage also notes a difference on the procedure: some say the lessor sells the shop first, then the hire is automatically rescinded; others say he rescinds first then sells. Regarding the phrase 'by clear evidence or by declaration', al-Ayni clarifies that 'ayan' means direct observation by the public, and 'bayan' means presenting evidence—so both are needed, not either/or. Finally, the text addresses the case of hiring a beast for travel. If the hirer decides not to travel, he may rescind because he would incur harm (e.g., losing the purpose of travel like hajj or seeking a creditor). If the animal-owner decides not to travel, he cannot rescind because he can delegate someone else. However, a narration from al-Karkhi allows rescission even for the animal-owner to prevent harm to the animal. The overall ruling: the right to rescind exists when an unforeseen harm arises that was not part of the contract, but the rescission may require judicial verification if the excuse is not obvious.

Key Terms
فسخ الإجارة (faskh al-ijarah): Rescission of the hire contract due to a valid excuse that prevents proceeding without additional harm.
عذر ('udhr): A valid excuse that makes continuation of the contract harmful beyond what was entitled.
قضاء (qada): A court order or judicial decree that permits rescission; required for hidden excuses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciled Hanafi position in the text, when is a court order (qada) required for rescission of a hire contract?
A) For apparent excuses only.
B) For hidden excuses only.
C) For all excuses.
D) For no excuses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi, rescission of hire is allowed for valid excuses; the need for a court order depends on whether the excuse is apparent or hidden.

Inter-Madhhab Dispute: Rescission of Hire for Excuses in Hanafi and Shafi'i Law
Presents Hanafi permissibility of rescission for excuses versus Shafi'i restriction to defect only

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on rescission of hire for excuses, identify the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the analogical reasoning (qiyas) that distinguishes the two schools.

Core Idea
Hire is rescindable for excuses because benefits arise moment by moment — unlike sale where only defects matter.

This passage from al-Ayni’s commentary on al-Kanz presents a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. At issue is whether a hire contract can be rescinded for supervening excuses beyond defects. The Hanafi school, relying on istihsan and the nature of benefits, permits rescission for a range of excuses. The Shafi'i school, analogizing hire to sale, restricts rescission to defect cases. Understanding this difference reveals how legal reasoning (talil) shapes contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school allows rescission of hire contracts for valid excuses, such as change of travel plans, bankruptcy, or the curing of a sickness that motivated the hire.
The passage begins by affirming 'these mentioned things are all excuses, and the hire is rescinded by them.'
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the opposite view: rescission of hire is only permitted for a defect in the subject matter, not for supervening excuses.
The text says 'wa qala al-Shafi'i: la tufsakhu bi al-a'dhar illa bi al-'ayb.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: benefits (manafi') are considered like physical objects (a'yan); therefore a hire contract is essentially a sale of benefits. In a sale, rescission is allowed only for defects (khiyar al-'ayb), not for subsequent excuses.
The text states 'li-anna al-manafi'a 'indahu bi-manzilat al-a'yan fa-takun ka al-bay' haqiqatan.'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi counter-argument: benefits are not like physical objects that exist at the time of contract; they are created gradually moment by moment. Hence an excuse that prevents future benefit is analogous to a defect discovered before taking possession in a sale, which does give a right to rescind.
The text says 'wa 'indana: hiya 'aqd 'ala al-manfa'ah bi-'awad, wa innaha tan'aqidu sa'atan fa-sa'atan 'ala hasab al-huduth, fa-sara al-'udhr fiha ka al-'ayb qabl al-qabd fi al-bay'.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, a hirer can cancel the contract without penalty if a valid excuse arises; in the Shafi'i school, the hirer remains liable unless a defect in the hired object exists.
For example, if a man hires a car for travel and then has a medical emergency, Hanafi allows rescission; Shafi'i does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العيني: فهذه الأشياء المذكورة كلها عذر، فتفسخ بها الإجارة، وقال الشافعي: لا تفسخ بالأعذار إلا بالعيب؛ لأن المنافع عنده بمنزلة الأعيان فتكون كالبيع حقيقة، وعندنا: هي عقد على المنفعة بعوض، وإنها تنعقد ساعة فساعة على حسب الحدوث، فصار العذر فيها كالعيب قبل القبض في البيع، فتفسخ به. للسفر: فإن لم يترك السفر ولكن وجد أرخص منه فهذا ليس بعذر فبدا له أي ظهر للمستأجر ما يوجب المنع من السفر. لا للمكاري: أي لا يكون عذرا إن بدا للمكاري رأي يمنع من السفر.
Al-Ayni said: So these mentioned things are all excuses, and the hire is rescinded by them. And al-Shafi'i said: It is not rescinded by excuses except for defect; because in his view, benefits are like physical objects, so it becomes like a sale in reality. And in our view, it is a contract on benefit for compensation, and it is concluded moment by moment according to occurrence, so the excuse in it is like a defect before taking possession in a sale, so it is rescinded by it. Regarding travel: if he did not abandon travel but found a cheaper alternative, this is not an excuse. 'It appeared to him' (fada lahu) means the hirer encountered something necessitating prevention of travel. 'Not to the animal-owner' means it is not an excuse if it appears to the animal-owner a view preventing travel.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the crux of the inter-madhhab dispute regarding rescission of hire contracts. Al-Ayni summarizes the Hanafi position: all the examples mentioned earlier (pain subsiding, divorce, bankruptcy, debt, change of travel plan) are valid excuses that allow rescission. Then he states the Shafi'i position: rescission of hire is only allowed for a defect in the subject matter, not for other excuses. The reasoning behind the difference is rooted in the legal nature of the hire contract. Al-Shafi'i analogizes hire to sale: benefits in his view are considered like physical objects (a'yan), so the contract of hire is like a sale of those benefits. In sale, the right to rescind is limited to defects (khiyar al-'ayb) and does not extend to supervening excuses (like change of circumstances) unless the contract itself provides for it. Hence, in Shafi'i law, once the contract is concluded, it is binding; only a defect in the benefit that existed at the time of contract can give rescission. The Hanafi position, on the other hand, treats hire as a contract on benefits (manafi') that come into existence gradually over time. Benefits are not like physical objects that exist at the moment of contract; they are created moment by moment. Therefore, the contract is akin to successive independent contracts each moment. An excuse that prevents the benefit from being useful in the future is analogous to a defect discovered in a purchased item before taking possession, which gives the buyer the right to rescind. Because taking possession in sale finalizes the transfer of risk; before possession, the seller bears the risk, and a defect allows rescission. Similarly, in hire, before the benefit is delivered (i.e., before the time period starts), an excuse allows rescission. After the benefit has been partially delivered, rescission may be limited. The text then clarifies an additional point: if the hirer decides not to travel but could find a cheaper alternative, that is not an excuse—it does not cause additional harm entitled by the contract. Also, if the animal-owner has a change of heart, that is not an excuse for him to rescind, because he does not suffer the same hardship. The essence: Hanafi uses istihsan (juristic preference) and the nature of benefits to allow broader rescission; Shafi'i adheres strictly to analogy with sale.

Key Terms
عذر ('udhr): A supervening circumstance that makes contract performance harmful; Hanafi considers it a cause for rescission.
المنافع (al-manafi'): Benefits are the subject matter of hire; they are non-physical, created over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core reason the Hanafi school allows rescission of hire for excuses beyond defects?
A) They consider hire a form of sale
B) They view benefits as created moment by moment, so excuses are like defects before possession
C) They rely on a specific hadith
D) They follow analogy with gifts

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, hire contracts are rescindable for genuine excuses because benefits are created over time, making it distinct from sale where only defects give rescission.

Rescission of Lease Due to Excuses: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on rescission of lease for excuses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on rescission of lease due to excuses and compare it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the key difference in the nature of manfa'ah.

Core Idea
A lease is rescinded by excuses because benefits arise moment by moment; Shafi'i treats benefits as objects, preventing rescission.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the rescission of a lease (ijara) when an excuse (udhr) arises after the contract is formed. The Hanafi school allows rescission for genuine excuses such as illness or change of travel plans, while the Shafi'i school restricts rescission to defects in the leased asset itself. The dispute hinges on the legal nature of the benefit (manfa'ah) that is the subject of the lease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its justification
State that the Hanafi school permits rescission of a lease when the lessee has a valid excuse (udhr) such as illness or change of travel plans. The justification is that benefits (manafi') are created moment by moment, so an excuse preventing benefit is like a defect discovered before possession in a sale.
The text explicitly mentions excuses like 'he changed his mind about traveling' or 'he fell ill'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its justification
State that Imam al-Shafi'i does not allow rescission for excuses; only a defect in the leased asset itself justifies rescission. His justification is that benefits are analogous to physical objects (a'yan), so the lease is a sale of usufruct and cannot be rescinded except for defects like in a sale of goods.
The text says benefits are 'bimanzilati al-a'yan' (like physical objects) in the Shafi'i view.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument
The Hanafi school responds that benefits are not physical objects; they come into existence gradually over time. Therefore, the contract is renewed each moment. An excuse that prevents the benefit from arising is analogous to a latent defect before possession, which allows rescission.
This is the core analytical difference: the nature of manfa'ah.
4
Apply the distinction to specific cases
Consider the examples given: lessee hires an animal for travel then changes mind (excuse), lessor changes mind (not an excuse). Also note Al-Karkhi's narration that the lessor may have an excuse if he faces hardship. Distinguish between the lessee's and lessor's positions.
The text emphasizes that the lessee suffers direct hardship, while the lessor can send another.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The thamarah is clear: Under Hanafi, the lessee can rescind for excuses; under Shafi'i, he cannot. This affects real-life contracts like hiring transportation for Hajj or medical treatment.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi is the position allowing rescission for the lessee's valid excuses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسخ الإجارة
أو بإقرار ولا مال له سواه أو استأجر دابة للسفر فبدا له منه لا للمكاري.
من المؤجر
للمؤجر الحانوت
أو بإقرار إلخ الظاهر أن المراد بالإقرار السابق على الإجارة وإلا يلزم أن يكون حجة متعدية. (ملا مسكين) أو استأجر إلخ: يعني لو استأجر دابة يسافر عليها، ثم بدا له أن لا يسافر، فهو عذر يفسخ به الإجارة، ولو بدا للمكاري لا يعذر؛ لأن المستأجر يلزمه ضرر ومشقة، وربما يفوته ما قصد كالحج وطلب الغريم، والمكاري لا يلزمه ذلك الضرر؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يقعد ويرسل غيره، وكذا لو مرض؛ لما ذكرنا، وروى الكرخي أنه عذر في حق المكاري؛ لأنه لا يعرو عن ضرر؛ ولأن غيره لا يشفق على دوابه مثله تكملة البحر الرائق: ٧٥/٨] قال العيني: فهذه الأشياء المذكورة كلها عذر، فتفسخ بها الإجارة، وقال الشافعي: لا تفسخ بالأعذار إلا بالعيب؛ لأن المنافع عنده بمنزلة الأعيان فتكون كالبيع حقيقة، وعندنا: هي عقد على المنفعة بعوض، وإنها تنعقد ساعة فساعة على حسب الحدوث، فصار العذر فيها كالعيب قبل القبض في البيع، فتفسخ به. للسفر: فإن لم يترك السفر ولكن وجد أرخص منه فهذا ليس بعذر فبدا له أي ظهر للمستأجر ما يوجب المنع من السفر. لا للمكاري: أي لا يكون عذرا إن بدا للمكاري رأي يمنع من السفر.
Chapter of Lease: Rescission of Lease. Or by acknowledgment and he has no property other than it, or he hired an animal for travel then he changed his mind about it, not for the lessor. For the lessor, the shop. 'Or by acknowledgment etc.' Apparently what is meant is an acknowledgment prior to the lease, otherwise it would become a transitive proof. Likewise if he falls ill, due to what we mentioned. Al-Ayni said: All these mentioned things are excuses, so the lease is rescinded by them. Al-Shafi'i said: It cannot be rescinded by excuses except by defect, because benefits to him are like physical objects, so it is like a sale in reality. 'Then it appears to him': meaning something appears to the lessee that necessitates prevention from travel. Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether a lease (ijara) can be rescinded due to excuses (a'dhar) that arise after the contract. The author begins by presenting specific excuses: the lessee (musta'jir) changes his mind about travel or falls ill, or the lessor confesses prior to the lease. The Hanafi position is that such excuses justify rescission, provided they are genuine hardships for the lessee. For example, if the lessee hires an animal for Hajj and then is prevented, the lease is rescinded. However, if the lessor merely finds a cheaper option, it is not an excuse. The commentary distinguishes between the lessee's change of mind (excuse) and the lessor's change of mind (not an excuse), reasoning that the lessee suffers direct harm, while the lessor can hire someone else. Al-Karkhi even extends the excuse to the lessor if he faces hardship. Al-Ayni confirms the Hanafi view. The dispute with Shafi'i is then articulated: Shafi'i considers benefits (manafi') like physical objects, so the lease is analogous to a sale, which cannot be rescinded except for defects. The Hanafi school argues that benefits come into existence moment by moment, so the contract is renewed each instant. Thus, an excuse that prevents the benefit from arising is like a defect before possession in a sale, justifying rescission. The key distinction is the nature of the subject matter: Shafi'i views it as a sale of usufruct, while Hanafis view it as a contract on successive benefits.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): An unforeseen circumstance that prevents the lessee from utilizing the leased benefit as intended, such as change of travel plans or illness.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or termination of a contract due to a legal cause arising after formation.
منفعة (manfa'ah): The usufruct or benefit that is the subject of the lease, which is created moment by moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the contract is void from the start
B) Because benefits are created moment by moment, so an excuse is like a defect before possession
C) Because the lessor always has the right to rescind
D) Because all contracts can be rescinded for any reason

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows rescission of lease for genuine excuses because benefits are created gradually; Shafi'i disagrees because he treats benefits as physical objects.

Liability for Fire: The Principle of Taseeb vs Mubashara
Ruling with proof on indirect causation and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a lessee/borrower who burns stubble and the fire spreads, and explain the distinction between direct (mubashara) and indirect (taseeb) causation in determining liability.

Core Idea
Indirect causation (taseeb) without transgression does not incur liability; only direct action or indirect with transgression does.

This passage presents a key Hanafi principle in tort liability: the distinction between direct and indirect causation. It uses the example of burning stubble on leased land to show that when the wind carries the fire, the lessee is not liable because his action is indirect and he has the right to burn. Understanding this distinction is foundational for many liability cases in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is burning stubble (حصائد) on leased or borrowed land. The ruling is that the lessee/borrower is not liable (لا يضمن) if the fire spreads and damages another's property.
The ruling applies only when the fire spreads via wind to another's land.
2
Determine the type of causation
The text classifies the act as taseeb (تسبيب), i.e., indirect causation, because the immediate cause of damage is the wind, not the act of burning. Liability for taseeb requires transgression (تعدي), which is absent here since the lessee has the right to burn on that land.
Contrast with mubashara (مباشرة) – direct action, where liability does not require transgression.
3
Compare with analogous cases
The text draws an analogy: digging a well on one's own property (taseeb, no liability) vs. shooting an arrow (mubashara, liability). The well case is the asl (original case) for the qiyas; burning stubble is the far' (new case). The illa is that both are taseeb without ta'addi.
The well is on one's own property; the stubble burning is on land where one has a right to use (lease/borrow).
4
Note the condition of wind
Al-Sarakhsi adds that if the wind was calm when the fire was lit and later changed, there is no liability. But if the wind was already turbulent, the person should have foreseen the spread and is liable.
This shows that taseeb liability can arise if there is negligence (ta'addi by omission).
5
Conclude the principle
The core principle: where an act is indirect and the actor has a right to perform it, no liability attaches absent transgression. Direct acts incur liability even without transgression.
This principle is widely applied in Hanafi tort law (e.g., hiring, borrowing, public land use).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أحرق حصائد أرض مستأجرة أو مستعارة، فاحترق شيء في أرض غيره المستأجر لم يضمن، وإن أقعد خياط أو صباغ في حانوته من عليه حصائد مفعول أقعد بسببه جمع حصيدة وهي ما يحصد من الزرع أو النبات عيني) أو مستعارة: أي أحرق المستعير حصائد أرض مستعارة. (عيني) لم يضمن إلخ أي لا يضمن المستأجر والمستعير ما احترق في أرض غيره إذا أحرق الحصائد أي بقايا أصول قصب محصود في أرض مستأجرة أو مستعارة؛ لأن هذا تسبيب وشرط الضمان التعدي، ولم يوجد، كمن حفر بئرا في ملكه فتلف به إنسان، بخلاف ما إذا رمى سهما في ملكه فأصاب إنسانا؛ لأنه مباشر، فلا يشترط فيه التعدي، قال السرخسي : هذا إذا كانت الريح هادئة أي ساكنة حين أوقد النار، ثم تغيرت، فأما إذا كانت مضطربة ينبغي أن يضمن وفي التمرتاشي : لو وضع جمرة في الطريق، فأحرقت شيئا ضمن؛ لأنه متعد، ولو وضع ورفعته الريح إلى شيء فأحرقته لا يضمن؛ لأن الريح نسخت فعله رمز الحقائق: ۲۸۸/۲] وإنما وضع المسألة في الأرض المستأجرة والمستعارة دون المملوكة؛ لأنه لما لم يضمن هنا، فعدم الضمان بالإحراق في أرضه أولى، قال في "الفتح": ومثل الأرض المستأجرة والمستعارة أرض بيت المال المعدة لحط القوافل والأحمال ومرعى الدواب وطرح الحصائد في أنه لا يضمن من الإحراق فيه إذا احترق به شيء في أرض غيره، وحاصله: أنه إن لم يكن له حق الانتفاع في أرض يضمن ما أحرقته بنفس الوضع إلا ما نقلته الريح على ما عليه الفتوى؛ لأن الريح نسخت فعله. (ملتقط من التكملة، الفتح)
And if he burns the stubble in leased or borrowed land, and something catches fire in another's land, the lessee/borrower is not liable. 'And if he seats a tailor or dyer in his shop' - 'stubble' is the object of 'seats'? 'Because of it'? - plural of 'hasida' which is what is harvested from crops or plants. (Ayni) 'Or borrowed': meaning the borrower burns stubble in borrowed land. (Ayni) 'did not guarantee etc.': meaning the lessee and borrower do not guarantee what is burned in another's land when they burn the stubble, i.e., the remaining stalks of harvested sugarcane in leased or borrowed land, because this is taseeb (indirect causation) and the condition for liability is transgression (ta'addi), which is not present. Like someone who digs a well in his own property and a person falls into it, by contrast to if he shoots an arrow on his property and hits a person, because that is direct action (mubashara), so transgression is not a condition. Al-Sarakhsi said: This is when the wind was calm, i.e., still, when he lit the fire, then it changed. If it was turbulent, he should be liable. In al-Tamartashi: If he places a coal in the road and it burns something, he is liable because he is transgressor. If he places it and the wind lifts it to something and burns it, he is not liable, because the wind abrogated his action. Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/288. And the issue is presented regarding leased and borrowed land, not owned land, because if there is no liability here, then certainly there is no liability for burning in his own land. Al-Fath said: Similar to leased and borrowed land is land of the treasury prepared for unloading caravans and loads and pasturing animals and dumping stubble, in that one is not liable for burning in it if something catches fire in another's land. Summary: If he does not have a right of use in the land, he is liable for what his own placement burns, except what the wind carries, according to the fatwa; because the wind abrogated his action. (Compiled from al-Takmila and al-Fath)
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding liability for fire damage when a person burns stubble (hasâ'id) on land he has a right to use (leased or borrowed) and the fire spreads to another's property. The ruling is that the lessee or borrower is not liable for the damage. The reason given is that his action is taseeb (indirect causation), and liability requires transgression (ta'addi), which is absent because he has the right to burn on that land. This is compared to digging a well on one's own property: if someone falls in, the digger is not liable because it is indirect. However, if the action is direct (mubashara), like shooting an arrow that hits someone, liability arises regardless of transgression. Al-Sarakhsi qualifies the ruling: if the wind was calm when the fire was lit and later changed, the person is not liable; but if the wind was already turbulent (mudtaribah), he should be liable because he should have foreseen the spread. Al-Tamartashi adds that placing a coal in the road (without right) makes one liable because it is transgression; but if the wind carries it, liability is removed because the wind abrogates the act. The text notes that the issue is set in leased/borrowed land (where one has the right to burn) rather than owned land, because if there is no liability here, certainly there is no liability on one's own land. Al-Fath extends the same rule to public treasury lands used for unloading caravans, pasturing animals, and dumping stubble. The final summary: one who has no right to use the land is liable for what his own placement burns, except what the wind carries (as per the fatwa). The key principle is the distinction between direct and indirect causation, and the role of transgression.

Key Terms
تسبيب (taseeb): Indirect causation, where the actor sets a condition but the immediate cause is external (e.g., wind).
مباشرة (mubashara): Direct action, where the actor directly causes harm (e.g., shooting an arrow).
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression, acting without right or beyond what is permitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a lessee not liable for fire damage when burning stubble on leased land and wind carries the fire to a neighbor's property?
A) Because he has permission from his landlord
B) Because the action is direct (mubashara) and no transgression is needed
C) Because the action is indirect (taseeb) and there is no transgression
D) Because the neighbor has no right to claim

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between direct and indirect causation: no liability for indirect acts without transgression, but unlimited liability for direct acts.

Qualifications and Extensions: Wind, Public Land, and the Mufta Bihi
Expansion probe detailing wind qualifications, public land extension, and final fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qualifications for liability in fire damage cases, including wind conditions, public land, and the mufta bihi regarding wind abrogating the actor's deed.

Core Idea
Liability for indirect fire damage depends on wind conditions and the right to use the land; the wind can abrogate the actor's deed and remove liability.

The previous lesson established the basic principle of taseeb vs mubashara. This lesson delves into the qualifications and extensions provided by later Hanafi scholars. Al-Sarakhsi and al-Tamartashi refine the rule by considering wind conditions and the distinction between lawful and unlawful placement. The text also extends the ruling to public treasury lands and concludes with the fatwa that wind abrogates the actor's action, thereby removing liability.

Guided Path
1
Review the basic ruling
Recall: A lessee/borrower burning stubble is not liable when wind carries fire to neighbor's land because of taseeb without ta'addi.
This ruling is the foundation for the qualifications.
2
Analyze the wind condition qualification (al-Sarakhsi)
Al-Sarakhsi distinguishes: if the wind was calm when the fire was lit and later changed, no liability. But if the wind was already turbulent, the person should have foreseen the risk and is liable.
This introduces a nuance: taseeb can become liable if there is negligence (a form of ta'addi by omission).
3
Analyze the coal-in-road example (al-Tamartashi)
Al-Tamartashi gives two scenarios: (a) placing coal in the road (transgression) – liable; (b) placing coal and wind carries it – not liable because wind abrogates the act.
This illustrates that wind can nullify the causal link if the initial placement was lawful (no ta'addi). If placement is unlawful, wind does not remove liability.
4
Extend the ruling to public land (al-Fath)
Al-Fath extends the same ruling to land of the treasury (بيت المال) used for caravans, pasturing, and dumping stubble. Those with a right to use such land are not liable for wind-carried fire.
This shows the principle is not limited to lease/borrow but applies to any lawful use.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text concludes: one without a right to use the land is liable for what his own placement burns, except what the wind carries – that is the fatwa (على ما عليه الفتوى). The reasoning: the wind abrogated his action.
The mufta bihi is that wind abrogates the actor's deed, removing liability even if the placement was wrongful? Actually careful: The text says for one without right, he is liable for his own placement (direct) but not for what wind carries. So the wind abrogation applies even when there is transgression? This is a nuance: if the placement is wrongful, he is liable for the placement itself (if it directly burns), but if the wind carries the fire elsewhere, the wind intervenes and he is not liable for that remote damage. This is the fatwa.

(عيني) لم يضمن إلخ أي لا يضمن المستأجر والمستعير ما احترق في أرض غيره إذا أحرق الحصائد أي بقايا أصول قصب محصود في أرض مستأجرة أو مستعارة؛ لأن هذا تسبيب وشرط الضمان التعدي، ولم يوجد، كمن حفر بئرا في ملكه فتلف به إنسان، بخلاف ما إذا رمى سهما في ملكه فأصاب إنسانا؛ لأنه مباشر، فلا يشترط فيه التعدي، قال السرخسي : هذا إذا كانت الريح هادئة أي ساكنة حين أوقد النار، ثم تغيرت، فأما إذا كانت مضطربة ينبغي أن يضمن وفي التمرتاشي : لو وضع جمرة في الطريق، فأحرقت شيئا ضمن؛ لأنه متعد، ولو وضع ورفعته الريح إلى شيء فأحرقته لا يضمن؛ لأن الريح نسخت فعله رمز الحقائق: ۲۸۸/۲] وإنما وضع المسألة في الأرض المستأجرة والمستعارة دون المملوكة؛ لأنه لما لم يضمن هنا، فعدم الضمان بالإحراق في أرضه أولى، قال في "الفتح": ومثل الأرض المستأجرة والمستعارة أرض بيت المال المعدة لحط القوافل والأحمال ومرعى الدواب وطرح الحصائد في أنه لا يضمن من الإحراق فيه إذا احترق به شيء في أرض غيره، وحاصله: أنه إن لم يكن له حق الانتفاع في أرض يضمن ما أحرقته بنفس الوضع إلا ما نقلته الريح على ما عليه الفتوى؛ لأن الريح نسخت فعله. Shirka al-Sana'i: Valid Partnership by Istihsan
Validates a profit-sharing arrangement between a shop owner and an artisan as a partnership by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a profit-sharing arrangement between a shop owner and an artisan as a partnership (shirka al-sana'i) using istihsan, and distinguish it from an invalid lease (ijarah) by identifying the key difference in legal categorization.

Core Idea
Seating a skilled artisan to share earnings is a valid partnership by istihsan, despite qiyas suggesting it is a void lease.

This passage addresses a common commercial arrangement: a shop owner seats a tailor or dyer in his shop, and they agree to share the earnings from customers' work equally. The author explains why this is not a lease with an unknown wage (which would be invalid) but a valid partnership of craftsmen (shirka al-sana'i) by virtue of istihsan. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi fiqh uses custom and legal preference to uphold practical business arrangements that strict analogy might invalidate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Read the two scenarios: (1) shop owner with prestige (jah) but no skill seats a skilled artisan; (2) shop owner with skill but no reputation seats a known but unskilled person. Note that in both cases the earnings are split equally.
Both scenarios are considered valid, but al-Binayah deems the first more appropriate.
2
Recognize the Qiyas that Would Invalidate
Understand the analogy: the arrangement resembles an ijarah (lease of services) where the wage is a portion of the output, which is majhul (unknown) at contract time. This is analogous to hiring a miller for a portion of the flour (qafiz al-tahan), which is invalid.
The illa (effective cause) is the uncertainty of the wage.
3
Understand the Istihsan Ground
Analyze why istihsan overrides qiyas: the contract is not an ijarah but a partnership (shirka). In shirka al-sana'i, both parties contribute to the work—one with skill (sina'a), the other with prestige or shop (jah). Custom (urf) treats this as a valid partnership, not a lease.
The basis is that there is a path to validity (sabil ila al-jawaz) and the practice is customary (muta'arif).
4
Derive the Hanafi Conclusion
Conclude that the contract is sahih (valid) by istihsan. The work is wajib (obligatory) on both parties, and the earnings are divided as agreed. The legal categorization as shirkah avoids the problem of unknown wage in ijarah.
The ruling is mufta bihi and recognized in later Hanafi works.
5
Note the Preference from al-Binayah
Al-Binayah in his commentary mentions that the first scenario (owner with jah, artisan skilled) is 'ashbah' (more appropriate), but both scenarios are considered valid. This shows an intra-Hanafi nuance but no disagreement on validity.
The first scenario is more typical of shirka al-sana'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أقعد خياط أو صباغ في حانوته من عليه خياطا أو صباغا على أن يتقبل الخياط الذي أقعد في حانوته خياطا العمل، وذلك بأن كان صاحب الدكان ذا جاه، ولا حذاقة له بالصنعة فأقعد من يعلم ويطرح عليه أي صاحب الدكان العمل، ويكون الأجر بينهما نصفين، ويجوز أن يكون الضمير في "يتقبل" راجعا للخياط الذي لا حانوت له، وذلك بأن كان صاحب الدكان حاذقا ، ولكنه غير معروف لا يؤتمن ولا يقصد، فأقعد على دكانه معروفا غير حاذق ليطرح هذا الذي قعد على صاحب الدكان العمل بالنصف. وقال في "البناية" والأول أشبه فهذه الصورة صحيحة استحسانا وكان القياس أن لا يجوز؛ لأنه استأجر بنصف ما يخرج من عمله، وهو مجهول كقفيز الطحان وجه الاستحسان أن هذه شركة الصنائع وليست بإجارة؛ لأن تفسير شركة الصنائع أن يكون العمل عليهما وإن كان أحدهما متولي العمل بحذاقته، والآخر متولي العمل بوجاهته، وإذا وجد ما له سبيل إلى الجواز وهو متعارف وجب القول بصحته، فيكون العمل واجبا عليهما، والأجر بينهما على ما عرف في موضعه. (ملخص الشروح)
And if he seats a tailor or a dyer in his shop, upon him a tailor or dyer, on condition that the tailor who is seated in his shop accepts the work, and this is when the shop owner has prestige (jah) but no skill in the craft, so he seats someone who knows, and the shop owner throws the work upon him, and the wage is half between them. And it is permissible that the pronoun in 'accepts' refers to the tailor who has no shop, and that is when the shop owner is skilled but unknown, not trusted, nor sought after, so he seats a known person who is not skilled on his shop so that this seated person throws the work upon the shop owner for half. And it is said in al-Binayah: 'The first is more appropriate.' So this picture is valid by istihsan, while the qiyas would be that it is not valid, because he hired him for half of what comes out of his work, which is unknown (majhul) like the miller's measure. The face of istihsan is that this is a partnership of craftsmen (shirka al-sana'i) and not a lease, because the interpretation of partnership of craftsmen is that the work is upon both, even if one undertakes the work by his skill and the other undertakes the work by his prestige. And when there is a way to validity and it is customary, it is necessary to rule for its validity. So the work is obligatory upon both, and the wage is between them as known in its place. (Summarized from the commentaries)
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a common commercial arrangement: a shop owner (sahib al-dukkan) seats a tailor or dyer in his shop to accept work from customers on the condition that they share the earnings equally. The author first describes two scenarios: (1) The shop owner has social prestige (jah) but lacks skill, so he seats a skilled artisan; the owner provides the location and generates business, and the artisan does the work, and they split the earnings. (2) The shop owner is skilled but unknown and untrusted, so he seats a known but unskilled person; the seated person relies on the owner's skill, and again they share. The ruling is that this arrangement is valid by istihsan, even though qiyas (analogy) would deem it invalid because it resembles an ijarah (lease) with an unknown wage (ajr majhul), like hiring a miller for a portion of the flour (qafiz al-tahan). The reason for istihsan is that this is not an ijarah but a partnership of craftsmen (shirka al-sana'i), where both parties contribute to the work: one with skill, the other with prestige or shop. Since the arrangement is customary (muta'arif) and there is a way to validity, it should be upheld as valid. Thus, the work is obligatory on both, and the division of earnings is as agreed. The author cites al-Binayah which prefers the first scenario as more appropriate, but both are valid. The key legal point is that the contract is categorized as a partnership, not a lease, avoiding the problem of unknown wage.

Key Terms
شركة الصنائع (shirka al-sana'i): A partnership where one partner contributes skill and the other contributes reputation or workspace, and they share the earnings.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference that overrides qiyas due to custom, necessity, or underlying equity.
جاه (jah): Social prestige or influence that attracts customers to the shop.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is seating a tailor in one's shop to share earnings halves considered valid by istihsan?
A) Because it is a form of ijarah with a known wage
B) Because it is a partnership of craftsmen (shirka al-sana'i), not a lease
C) Because the wage is predetermined
D) Because the shop owner bears all liability

Key Takeaway
Shirka al-sana'i is valid by istihsan because it is a partnership where both parties contribute, overcoming the qiyas that would make it an invalid lease.

6 min
Liability for Fire Spreading from Rented or Borrowed Land
Ruling on non-liability for indirect damage from fire in rented/borrowed land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for fire spreading from rented/borrowed land and identify the conditions under which liability arises using the distinction between direct and indirect causation.

Core Idea
Indirect causation (tasbīb) without transgression (ta'addī) incurs no liability, unlike direct action.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi tort law: the distinction between direct and indirect causation. The case involves a hirer or borrower who burns harvested crops on rented or borrowed land, and the fire spreads to another's property. The ruling hinges on whether the fire was directly caused or indirectly caused, and whether any transgression occurred. This distinction, along with the state of the wind, determines liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act? Burning harvested crops on rented/borrowed land. What is the ruling? The hirer/borrower is not liable (lā yadman) for damage to another's property.
The ruling is a hukm wad'ī (liability ruling), not a hukm taklīfī.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
The act is indirect causation (tasbīb). For tasbīb, liability requires transgression (ta'addī). In this case, there is no transgression because the fire was lit on land where the actor had a right to use (rented/borrowed).
Contrast with direct action (mubāshara), where transgression is not required.
3
Examine the analogies
The author compares to digging a well on one's own property (no liability for resulting death) because it is tasbīb without transgression. Contrast with shooting an arrow on one's own land that hits someone (liability) because it is mubāshara.
This is a qiyās that establishes the rule for tasbīb.
4
Note the condition of the wind
Al-Sarakhsī adds: if the wind is calm at ignition, no liability; if the wind is turbulent when the fire is lit, the actor is liable because the lighting itself is transgression. Al-Tamartāshī distinguishes: placing a coal on the road (transgression) leads to liability, but if wind carries it, no liability because the wind abrogates the act.
The state of the wind at the time of lighting is crucial.
5
Extend to public land
The same rule applies to land of the public treasury (bayt al-māl) set for unloading caravans, pasturing animals, and disposing of harvested crops

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أحرق حصائد أرض مستأجرة أو مستعارة، فاحترق شيء في أرض غيره لم يضمن، وإن أقعد خياط أو صباغ في حانوته من عليه بسببه (عيني) لم يضمن إلخ أي لا يضمن المستأجر والمستعير ما احترق في أرض غيره إذا أحرق الحصائد أي بقايا أصول قصب محصود في أرض مستأجرة أو مستعارة؛ لأن هذا تسبيب وشرط الضمان التعدي، ولم يوجد، كمن حفر بئرا في ملكه فتلف به إنسان، بخلاف ما إذا رمى سهما في ملكه فأصاب إنسانا؛ لأنه مباشر، فلا يشترط فيه التعدي، قال السرخسي: هذا إذا كانت الريح هادئة أي ساكنة حين أوقد النار، ثم تغيرت، فأما إذا كانت مضطربة ينبغي أن يضمن وفي التمرتاشي: لو وضع جمرة في الطريق، فأحرقت شيئا ضمن؛ لأنه متعد، ولو وضع ورفعته الريح إلى شيء فأحرقته لا يضمن؛ لأن الريح نسخت فعله رمز الحقائق: ۲۸۸/۲] وإنما وضع المسألة في الأرض المستأجرة والمستعارة دون المملوكة؛ لأنه لما لم يضمن هنا، فعدم الضمان بالإحراق في أرضه أولى، قال في "الفتح": ومثل الأرض المستأجرة والمستعارة أرض بيت المال المعدة لحط القوافل والأحمال ومرعى الدواب وطرح الحصائد في أنه لا يضمن من الإحراق فيه إذا احترق به شيء في أرض غيره، وحاصله: أنه إن لم يكن له حق الانتفاع في أرض يضمن ما أحرقته بنفس الوضع إلا ما نقلته الريح على ما عليه الفتوى؛ لأن الريح نسخت فعله.
If someone burns harvested crops of a rented or borrowed land, and something in another's land catches fire, he is not liable. And if a tailor or dyer is seated in his shop... (commentary) meaning the lessee or borrower does not guarantee what burns in another's land if he burns the harvested crops, i.e., remnants of harvested sugarcane stalks in rented or borrowed land; because this is causation (tasbīb) and the condition for liability is transgression (ta'addī), which is not present. Like one who digs a well on his property and a person perishes in it; unlike if he shoots an arrow on his property and it hits a person, because that is direct action (mubāshara), and transgression is not required. Al-Sarakhsī said: This applies when the wind is calm when he lights the fire, then it changes. But if the wind is turbulent, he should be liable. And in al-Tamartāshī: If one places a coal in the road and it burns something, he is liable because he is transgressor. If he places it and the wind lifts it to something and burns it, he is not liable because the wind abrogated his action. The issue is placed in rented and borrowed land, not owned land, because if he is not liable here, non-liability for burning in his own land is even more apt. In al-Fatḥ: Rented and borrowed land are like the land of the public treasury set for unloading caravans, pasture for animals, and disposal of harvested crops, in that one is not liable for burning in it if something in another's land burns. Summary: if one does not have a right of use in a land, he is liable for what his placement burns except what the wind carries, according to the fatwa, because the wind abrogated his action.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on liability for damage caused by fire that spreads from rented or borrowed land. The author establishes that a hirer or borrower who burns harvested crops on such land is not liable for fire that spreads to another's property. The key distinction is between indirect causation (tasbīb) and direct action (mubāshara). Liability requires transgression (ta'addī) in causation, but not in direct action. The example of digging a well on one's own property (no liability) versus shooting an arrow (liability) illustrates this. Al-Sarakhsī adds a condition: if the wind is calm when lighting the fire and then changes, no liability; but if the wind is turbulent when lighting, liability arises. Al-Tamartāshī distinguishes: placing a coal on the road is transgression, but if wind carries it, no liability as the wind abrogates the act. The ruling applies even more so to one's own land. The commentary then extends the same rule to public land designated for similar purposes.

Key Terms
تسبيب (tasbīb): Indirect causation of damage, as opposed to direct action (mubāshara).
مباشر (mubāshir): Direct agent of harm, where liability applies even without transgression.
تعدي (ta'addī): Transgression beyond legal bounds, a condition for liability in indirect causation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the condition for liability in a case of indirect causation (tasbīb) like burning crops in rented land?
A) The act must be done intentionally
B) There must be transgression (ta'addī)
C) The damage must be immediate
D) The property must be owned by the actor

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, indirect causation (tasbīb) of harm without transgression (ta'addī) does not incur liability, especially when the harm is carried by a later wind that abrogates the original act.

Hiring a Camel for a Palanquin and Two Riders: Validity by Istihsan
Inter-madhhab dispute on istihsan vs qiyas in hire contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school uses istihsan to validate a hire contract with minor uncertainty (jahalah yasirah) and distinguish it from cases of gross uncertainty (jahalah fahishah).

Core Idea
Uncertainty in a subsidiary term is overcome by custom; the main object known saves the contract.

In this passage, we encounter a classic Hanafi application of istihsan to override strict qiyas. The case involves hiring a camel to carry a palanquin and two riders, where the exact dimensions of the palanquin are not specified. The Hanafi school upholds the contract's validity by invoking custom (muta'āraf) to remove the ambiguity, while Imam al-Shafi'i rejects it due to jahālah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping Hanafi contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the disputed term
What is the hire contract? What is unspecified? The hire is for a camel to carry a palanquin (mihmal) and two riders; the palanquin's dimensions and exact load are not stated.
The rider is specified and known; the palanquin is a subsidiary (tābi).
2
Apply the qiyas ruling
Strict analogy (qiyas) would invalidate the contract due to uncertainty (jahālah) in the subject matter, as the palanquin's specifications are ambiguous.
This is the position of Imam al-Shafi'i.
3
Introduce istihsan
Hanafi istihsan overrides qiyas here because the primary object (the rider) is known, and the uncertainty in the palanquin is minor and removed by custom.
Custom (muta'āraf) supplies the standard load, cover, etc.
4
Distinguish from gross uncertainty
Contrast with hiring a donkey without specifying any load: there the uncertainty is gross (fāhish) because the type of load is unknown. Here the type (palanquin) is specified, so uncertainty is minor (yasīr).
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
Shafi'i invalidates entirely; Hanbali requires sight of the items; Hanafi holds it valid by istihsan and recommends seeing the items (mustahabb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن استأجر جملا ليحمل عليه محملا، وراكبين إلى مكة صح، العمل بالنصف صح، وإن استأجر جملا ليحمل عليه محملا، وراكبين إلى مكة صح، يقعدان فيه وليه الحمل المعتاد، ورؤيته أحب، والمقدار زاد فأكل منه رد عوضه، وتصح الإجارة وفسخها والمزارعة والمعاملة والمستأجر العمل: سواء اتحد العمل أو اختلف كخياط مع قصار (فتح) صح هذا الفعل استحسانا؛ لأن هذه شركة الصنائع لا إجارة. محملا بفتح الميم الأول وكسر الثاني أو بالعكس: الهودج الكبير. (مسكين) صح: أي استحسانا والقياس أن لا يجوز، وهو قول الإمام الشافعي؛ لأنه فيه جهالة، وجه الاستحسان أن المقصود هو الراكب وهو المعلوم، والمحمل تابع وما فيه من الجهالة تزول بالصرف إلى المتعارف وله المتعارف من الحمل والزاد والغطاء وغير ذلك مما هو معلوم عند أهل العرف، لا يقال: هذه متكررة مع قوله: وإن استأجر حمارا و لم يسم ما يحمل، قلنا: هناك لم يبين ما يحمل، فكانت الجهالة فاحشة وههنا بين ما يحمل فكانت يسيرة؛ لأنه بين الحمل، ولم يبين قدره. تكملة البحر الرائق: ۷۷/۸] (عيني) ورؤيته: أي رؤية المكاري المحمل والراكب وما يتبعهما. أحب لأنه أقرب لحصول الرضا، وعند أحمد: لا بدون المشاهدة.
If he hires a camel to carry on it a palanquin and two riders to Mecca, it is valid. The work for half is valid. And if he hires a camel to carry on it a palanquin and two riders to Mecca, it is valid; they sit in it, and upon him is the customary load, and seeing it is more beloved. And the amount of provisions: if he eats from it, he must return its equivalent. And the hire and its cancellation, muzara'ah, mu'amalah, and the hirer's work are valid: whether the work is uniform or different, like a tailor with a washer. This action is valid by istihsan; because it is a partnership of crafts, not a hire. 'Mihmal' (palanquin) is a large howdah. 'Valid' means by istihsan, and by qiyas it is not valid, and it is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i, because it contains uncertainty. The basis of istihsan is that the intended object is the rider, which is known, and the palanquin is subsidiary. The uncertainty is removed by referring to custom, and he is due the customary load, provisions, covering, etc., which are known among people of custom. It cannot be said that this is redundant with his statement: 'if he hires a donkey and does not specify what to carry'; we say: there he did not state what to carry, so the uncertainty was gross, whereas here he stated what to carry (a palanquin) so it is minor; he stated the type of load but not its quantity. Takmilat al-Bahr al-Rā'iq 8:77. (Aynī) 'And seeing him' i.e., seeing the carrier, the palanquin, the rider, and what accompanies them is more beloved because it is closer to achieving satisfaction. According to Ahmad: it is not valid without seeing.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the validity of hiring a camel to carry a palanquin (mihmal) and two riders to Mecca. The Hanafi position, by istihsan, is that the contract is valid, whereas the Qiyas position (attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i) is that it is invalid due to uncertainty (jahālah) in the description of the palanquin and its load. The basis of istihsan: the primary object is the rider (known and specified), and the palanquin is subsidiary. Any uncertainty in the palanquin's specifications is removed by referring to customary standards (muta'āraf) regarding load, provisions, covering, etc. The Hanafis distinguish this from the case of hiring a donkey without specifying what to carry, which is invalid because the uncertainty is gross. Here, the type of load is specified (a palanquin), so the uncertainty is minor and overcome by custom. Seeing the items is recommended (mustahabb) but not required; Ahmad ibn Hanbal requires actual sight.

Key Terms
جهالة (jahālah): Uncertainty or ambiguity in a contract term that may lead to dispute.
متعارف (muta'āraf): Customary practice among people in a given context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the contract is a gift
D) Because the palanquin is seen at the time of contract

Key Takeaway
Hanafi istihsan validates contracts with minor uncertainty in subsidiary terms when custom provides specification.
